Prompt: when does a dieback or population crash occur
Ground truth: In population dynamics and population ecology, overshoot occurs when a population temporarily exceeds the long term carrying capacity of its environment. The environment usually has mechanisms in place to prevent overshoot. For example, plants are only able to regenerate and regrow a few times after being consumed before completely dying off. The consequence of overshoot is called a collapse, a crash or a die-off in which there is a decline in population density. The entire sequence or trajectory undergone by the population and its environment together is often termed ' overshoot-and-collapse '.
[0.157, 0.298, 0.663] Agent refined response: According to the knowledge provided, a dieback or population crash occurs when the rate of reproduction is higher than the rate of death, leading to an increase in population density. This can happen when there are lag effects, meaning that the impact of environmental changes on the population is delayed, and reproduction rates remain high while the death rate increases. The entire sequence or trajectory undergoned by the population and its environment together can be termed an "overshoot-and-collapse," and can have severe impacts on entire ecosystems.

Prompt: what are the 3 layers of the meninges
Ground truth: The meninges (/məˈnɪndʒiːz/, singular: meninx (/ˈmiːnɪŋks/or/ˈmɛnɪŋks/), from Ancient Greek: μῆνιγξ, translit. mēninx, lit. ' membrane ', adjectival: meningeal/məˈnɪndʒəl/) are the three membranes that envelop the brain and spinal cord. In mammals, the meninges are the dura mater, the arachnoid mater, and the pia mater. Cerebrospinal fluid is located in the subarachnoid space between the arachnoid mater and the pia mater. The primary function of the meninges is to protect the central nervous system.

Prompt: where do you get male pattern baldness from
Ground truth: Male pattern hair loss is believed to be due to a combination of genetics and the male hormone dihydrotestosterone. The cause in female pattern hair loss remains unclear.
[0.026, 0.169, 0.581] Agent refined response: Male pattern baldness, also known as androgenetic alopecia, is a common condition in which men experience hair loss due to a combination of genetic and hormonal factors. It is caused by the presence of the androgen receptor gene, which is inherited from one's parents. As men age, the levels of the hormone dihydrotestosterone (DHT) in their bodies increase, leading to hair loss. DHT is a potent form of testosterone that is produced by the enzyme 5-alpha-reductase in the hair follicles. Over time, the hair follicles are shrunk, leading to a gradual reduction in hair growth and eventual baldness.

Other factors that can contribute to male pattern baldness include:

* Genetics: A family history of male pattern baldness increases the risk of developing the condition.
* Hormonal imbalances: Imbalances in hormone levels, such as an excess of DHT, can contribute to hair loss.

* Stress: Prolonged periods of stress can lead to an increase in the production of cortisol, a hormone that can contribute to hair loss.
* Poor diet: A diet lacking in essential nutrients, such as iron, zinc, and biotin, can contribute to hair loss.

Ground truth: Daylight saving time in the United States is the practice of setting the clock forward by one hour during the warmer part of the year, so that evenings have more daylight and mornings have less. Most areas of the United States observe daylight saving time (DST), the exceptions being Arizona (except for the Navajo, who do observe daylight saving time on tribal lands), Hawaii, and the overseas territories of American Samoa, Guam, the Northern Mariana Islands, Puerto Rico, and the United States Virgin Islands. The Uniform Time Act of 1966 established the system of uniform Daylight Saving Time throughout the US.

Prompt: where are the cones in the eye located
Ground truth: Cone cells, or cones, are one of three types of photoreceptor cells in the retina of mammalian eyes (e.g. the human eye). They are responsible for color vision and function best in relatively bright light, as opposed to rod cells, which work better in dim light. Cone cells are densely packed in the fovea centralis, a 0.3 mm diameter rod-free area with very thin, densely packed cones which quickly reduce in number towards the periphery of the retina. There are about six to seven million cones in a human eye and are most concentrated towards the macula. The commonly cited figure of six million cone cells in the human eye was found by Osterberg in 1935. Oyster's textbook (1999) cites work by Curcio et al. (1990) indicating an average close to 4.5 million cone cells and 90 million rod cells in the human retina.
[0.000, 0.237, 0.578] Agent refined response: According to the knowledge provided, cones are located in the following areas of the eye:

* The fovea: Cones are greatly outnumbered by rods in most parts of the Retina, but they are much more numerous in the fovea.
* The fovea is a small, specialized area in the center of the retina that is responsible for high-acuity vision.

So, the cones in the eye are primarily located in the fovea.

Prompt: when does april fools day end at noon
Ground truth: In Poland, prima aprilis ("1 April" in Latin) as a day of jokes is a centuries-long tradition. It is a day in which many jokes are told; various hoaxes--sometimes very sophisticated--are prepared by people, media (which often cooperate to make the "information" more credible) and even public institutions. Serious activities are usually avoided, and generally every word said on April 1st can be a lie, or a joke. The conviction for this is so strong that the Polish anti-Turkish alliance with Leopold I signed on April 1, 1683, was backdated to March 31. However, for some in Poland prima aprilis ends at noon of April 1st, and prima aprilis jokes after that hour are considered inappropriate and not classy.

It is a day when people in some European and Western countries commemorate by playing practical jokes and spread hoaxes, and the pranks are usually exposed by shouting "April Fool!" at the unfortunate victim. Newspapers, magazines, and other published media may also report fake stories, which are usually clarified the next day or in smaller letters below the news section.

Prompt: where is connective tissue found in the body
Ground truth: Connective tissue (CT) is one of the four basic types of animal tissue, along with epithelial tissue, muscle tissue, and nervous tissue. It develops from the mesoderm. Connective tissue is found in between other tissues everywhere in the body, including the nervous system. In the central nervous system, the three outer membranes (the meninges) that envelop the brain and spinal cord are composed of connective tissue. They support and protect the body. All connective tissue consists of three main components: fibers (elastic and collagenous fibers), ground substance and cells. Not all authorities include blood or lymph as connective tissue because they lack the fiber component. All are immersed in the body water.
[0.130, 0.258, 0.644] Agent refined response: Connective tissue is found throughout the body, including in the nervous system. In the central nervous system, the meninges, which are the three outer membranes that envelop the brain and spine, are composed of connective tissue and provide support and protection to the brain and spinal cord. Connective tissue is also found in other parts of the body, such as:

* Blood vessels: Connective tissue surrounds and supports blood vessels, including arteries, veins, and capillaries.
* Lymphatic system: Connective tissue forms the walls of lymph vessels and lymph nodes, and supports the immune system.
* Muscles: Connective tissue attaches muscles to bones and other structures, and provides support and stability to the musculoskeletal system.
* Bones: Connective tissue forms the bone matrix, which provides structural support and protection to the bone.
* Tendons and ligaments: Connective tissue connects muscles to bones and other tissues, and provides support and stability to joints.
* Aponeuroses: Connective tissue forms the membranes that support and separate muscles, and provide a framework for the musculoskeletal structure.
* Cornea: Connective tissue forms the cornea, the clear outer layer of the eye.
* Organs: Connective tissue supports and connects organs in the body, such as the liver, kidneys, and spleen.

In summary, connective tissue is found throughout the human body, and plays a crucial role in supporting and connecting various structures and organs, as well as providing protection and support to the nervous system.

Prompt: the elements in each period have the same number of
Ground truth: A period in the periodic table is one of the horizontal rows, all of those elements have the same number of electron shells. Going across a period, each element has one more proton and is less metallic than its predecessor. Arranged this way, groups of elements in the same column have similar chemical and physical properties, reflecting the periodic law. For example, the alkali metals lie in the first column (group 1) and share similar properties, such as high reactivity and the tendency to lose one electron to arrive at a noble-gas electronic configuration. As of 2016 a total of 118 elements have been discovered and confirmed.

Prompt: when does the heart develop and begin pumping blood
Ground truth: Heart development refers to the prenatal development of the human heart. This begins with the formation of two endocardial tubes which merge to form the tubular heart, also called the primitive heart tube, that loops and septates into the four chambers and paired arterial trunks that form the adult heart. The heart is the first functional organ in vertebrate embryos, and in the human, beats spontaneously by week 4 of development.
[0.167, 0.400, 0.708] Agent refined response: According to the knowledge provided, the heart begins to develop and pump blood during the prenatal stage of human development. Specifically, the heart develops from two endocardial tubes that merge to form the tubular heart or primitive heart tube, which loops and septates into the four-chambered heart by the end of the third week of embryonic development (around day 21).

So, the heart begins to develop and start pumping blood around day 21 of embryonic development, which is before the fetus is born.

Prompt: where does decussation occur in the spinothalamic pathway
Ground truth: The axons of the tract cells cross over (decussate) to the other side of the spinal cord via the anterior white commissure, and to the anterolateral corner of the spinal cord (hence the spinothalamic tract being part of the anterolateral system). Decussation usually occurs 1-2 spinal nerve segments above the point of entry. The axons travel up the length of the spinal cord into the brainstem, specifically the rostral ventromedial medulla.
[0.214, 0.366, 0.704] Agent refined response: According to the knowledge provided, decussation occurs 1-2 spinal nerve segments abovethe point of entry in the spinal cord. This means that the axons of the tract cells decussate, or cross over, to the other side of the spical cord at a point that is 1-2 segments above the location where the tract enters the spinal cord.

Prompt: when did new york stop using the electric chair
Ground truth: Capital punishment is not in force in the State of New York. The last execution took place in 1963, when Eddie Mays was electrocuted at Sing Sing Prison. The state was the first to adopt the electric chair as a method of execution, which replaced hanging. Following the U.S. Supreme Court's ruling declaring existing capital punishment statutes unconstitutional in Furman v. Georgia (1972), New York was without a death penalty until 1995, when then-Governor George Pataki signed a new statute into law, which provided for execution by lethal injection.

Prompt: what was the religion in the virginia colony
Ground truth: In the 1740s, the established Anglican church had about 70 parish priests around the colony. There was no bishop, and indeed, there was fierce political opposition to having a bishop in the colony. The Anglican priests were supervised directly by the Bishop of London. Each county court gave tax money to the local vestry, composed of prominent laymen. The vestry provided the priest a glebe of 200 or 300 acres (1.2 km), a house, and perhaps some livestock. The vestry paid him an annual salary of 16,000 pounds-of-tobacco, plus 20 shillings for every wedding and funeral. While not poor, the priests lived modestly and their opportunities for improvement were slim.
[0.000, 0.183, 0.529] Agent refined response: According to the knowledge provided, the religion in the Virginia Colony during the 18th century was primarily Anglican, also known as Episcopalian. However, after the American Revolution, many Anglican clergy and loyalists went into exile, and the local vestry no longer distributed funds or had local government functions such as providing poor relief.

In 1790, the Right Reverend James Madison, a cousin of Patriot James Madison, was appointed as the first Episcopal Bishop of Virginia, and he slowly rebuilt the denomiation within the framework of freedom of choice of belief and worship (see also Virginia Portal and History of Religion in the United States).

Prompt: when was the biltmore house opened to the public
Ground truth: In an attempt to bolster the estate's financial situation during the Great Depression, Cornelia and her husband opened Biltmore to the public in March 1930 at the request of the City of Asheville, which hoped the attraction would revitalize the area with tourism. Biltmore closed during World War II and in 1942, 62 paintings and 17 sculptures were moved to the estate by train from the National Gallery of Art in Washington, D.C. to protect them in the event of an attack on the United States. The Music Room on the first floor was never finished, so it was used for storage until 1944, when the possibility of an attack became more remote. Among the works stored were the Gilbert Stuart portrait of George Washington and works by Rembrandt, Raphael, and Anthony van Dyck. David Finley, the gallery director, was a friend of Edith Vanderbilt and had stayed at the estate.
[0.000, 0.107, 0.476] Agent refined response: According to the knowledge provided, Biltmore House was opened to the public in March 1930.

Prompt: what do the 3 dots mean in math
Ground truth: In logical argument and mathematical proof, the therefore sign (∴) is generally used before a logical consequence, such as the conclusion of a syllogism. The symbol consists of three dots placed in an upright triangle and is read therefore. It is encoded at U + 2234 ∴ therefore (HTML & # 8756; &there4;). For common use in Microsoft Office hold the ALT key and type "8756". While it is not generally used in formal writing, it is used in mathematics and shorthand. It is complementary to U + 2235 ∵ because (HTML & # 8757;).
[0.000, 0.165, 0.497] Agent refined response: In addition to also appearing in several other contexts, the number also appears in textbooks to signify "three" but not, as in the United States, in conjunction with "thousand"; this is because three is the first digit to appear more than once in a number in the English language. This is a more recent development in the 20th century; in the 19th century, the third digit (3) was not a number to be used in place of a thousand. This is why in 1950s England there is a famous comic strip called The Three Musketeers. In the United States, the number three is not used in this way.

Prompt: who was the first chief minister of west bengal
Ground truth: Since 1947, there have been eight Chief Ministers of West Bengal. The first was Prafulla Chandra Ghosh of the Indian National Congress, who was succeeded by his party-mates Dr Bidhan Chandra Roy and Prafulla Chandra Sen. A decade of instability followed, marred by fractious coalition governments and frequent impositions of President's rule. The instability ended with 1977 election victory of the Communist Party of India (Marxist) (CPM). Headed by Jyoti Basu, the CPM-led Left Front government was in office for over 23 years (1977--2000). Left rule in West Bengal continued for another 10 years under Buddhadeb Bhattacharya, before its defeat in the 2011 election by the Trinamool Congress. Appointed on 20 May 2011, Trinamool leader Mamata Banerjee is the current incumbent, the state's first woman chief minister.

In 1962, he became the second Chief Minister of West Bengal. In 2004, the West Bengal government named the state's first legislative assembly building after him. Prompt: what is the maximum data rate for the 802.11a standard select one
Ground truth: The 802.11 a standard uses the same data link layer protocol and frame format as the original standard, but an OFDM based air interface (physical layer). It operates in the 5 GHz band with a maximum net data rate of 54 Mbit/s, plus error correction code, which yields realistic net achievable throughput in the mid-20 Mbit/s.
[0.000, 0.197, 0.658] Agent refined response: In 802.11b / g and n, the maximum data rate has been 54 Mbps. The 802.11n standard has a maximum data rate of 600 Mbps, while the 802.1 11ac standard allows a maximum data rate of 1.3 Gbps (gigabit). The 802.1 2ay standard, however, does not allow a maximum data rate of 100 Mbps. The standard uses a 54 Mbps PHY (Physical layer) and 1544 Mbps OFDM (Orthogonal frequency division multiplexing) and uses the 5 GHz frequency band.

Prompt: where does the term dog and pony show come from
Ground truth: The term was originally used in the United States in the late-19th and early-20th centuries to refer to small traveling circuses that toured through small towns and rural areas. The name derives from the common use of performing dogs and ponies as the main attractions of the events. Performances were generally held in open-air arenas, such as race tracks or public spaces in localities that were too small or remote to attract larger, more elaborate performers or performances. It started small, but evolved into a full circus show. Other early dog and pony shows included Morris' Equine and Canine Paradoxes (1883) and Hurlburt's Dog and Pony Show (late 1880s).

Later generations would count this event, known as the hijra, as the start of the Islamic era.

Prompt: where does us highway 1 start and end
Ground truth: U.S. Route 1 (US 1) is a major north--south U.S. Highway that serves the East Coast of the United States. It runs 2,369 miles (3,813 km), from Fort Kent, Maine, at the Canada--US border, south to Key West, Florida, making it the longest north--south road in the United States. US 1 is generally paralleled by Interstate 95 (I-95), though the former is significantly farther west (inland) between Jacksonville, Florida, and Petersburg, Virginia. The highway connects most of the major cities of the East Coast--including Miami, Jacksonville, Richmond, Washington, D.C., Baltimore, Philadelphia, New York City and Boston, passing from the Southeastern United States to New England.

Prompt: the origins of the stations of the cross
Ground truth: The Stations of the Cross or the Way of the Cross, also known as the Way of Sorrows or the Via Crucis, refers to a series of images depicting Jesus Christ on the day of his crucifixion and accompanying prayers. The stations grew out of imitations of Via Dolorosa in Jerusalem which is believed to be the actual path Jesus walked to Mount Calvary. The object of the stations is to help the Christians faithful to make a spiritual pilgrimage through contemplation of the Passion of Christ. It has become one of the most popular devotions and the stations can be found in many Western Christian churches, including Anglican, Lutheran, Methodist, and Roman Catholic ones.
[0.074, 0.308, 0.591] Agent refined response: Of the Stations of the Cross stated in the Catholic Church tradition is the commemoration of the Passion of Jesus Christ as depicted in the Gospels of the New Testament: the sufferings and death of Jesus on the cross, as well as his removal from the cross and burial. Although the Stations of the Cross are not explicitly mentioned in the Bible, the practice of following the path of the Via Dolorosa (the route from the Garden of Gethsemane to the Church of the Holy Sepulchre) is considered to be of apostolic origin.

Prompt: when does a limit of a function not exist
Ground truth: Formal definitions, first devised in the early 19th century, are given below. Informally, a function f assigns an output f (x) to every input x. We say the function has a limit L at an input p: this means f (x) gets closer and closer to L as x moves closer and closer to p. More specifically, when f is applied to any input sufficiently close to p, the output value is forced arbitrarily close to L. On the other hand, if some inputs very close to p are taken to outputs that stay a fixed distance apart, we say the limit does not exist.
[0.000, 0.131, 0.538] Agent refined response: As an infinite series may be defined as a limit of a function, an infinite series may not converge. As such, the limit of a function (e.g. a function (x → 0) → 0) does not exist, if the function does not converge. A function may not converge if it has a divergent infinite series (e.g. 1 + 1 + 1 + ⋯ (infinite)) as a Taylor series. It may also not converge if the function does not have a Taylor series (e.g. the Cantor function). A function may also not have a limit if it has a discontinuity (e.g. the function (x → 0) has a discontinuity at x = 0).

Prompt: when did beryl markham fly across the atlantic
Ground truth: When Markham decided to take on the Atlantic crossing, no female pilot had yet flown non-stop from Europe to New York, and no woman had made the westward flight solo, though several had died trying. Markham hoped to claim both records. On 4 September 1936, she took off from Abingdon, England. After a 20-hour flight, her Vega Gull, The Messenger, suffered fuel starvation due to icing of the fuel tank vents, and she crash-landed at Baleine Cove on Cape Breton Island, Nova Scotia, Canada. She became the first person to make it from England to North America non-stop from east to west. She was celebrated as an aviation pioneer.
[0.061, 0.234, 0.608] Agent refined response: Because of her early death, Markham never achieved her ambition of flying solo across the Atlantic. She made a non-stop east-west flight from Darwin to Karachi, on a 1936 Percival Vega, and made a non-stop flight from Nairobi to Cairo on a 1939 de Havilland Gypsy Moth (the first woman to make the journey). Markham also made the first solo transcontinental flight from east to west across Africa. She also set the first woman-only record for solo flight across the Atlantic from east to west. Markham was the first woman to be granted the Alamein Flying Cup.

Prompt: where does eye of the round roast come from
Ground truth: A round steak is a beef steak from the "round", the rear leg of the cow. The round is divided into cuts including the eye (of) round, bottom round, and top round, with or without the "round" bone (femur), and may include the knuckle (sirloin tip), depending on how the round is separated from the loin. This is a lean cut and it is moderately tough. Lack of fat and marbling makes round dry out when cooked with dry-heat cooking methods like roasting or grilling. Round steak is commonly prepared with slow moist-heat methods including braising, to tenderize the meat and maintain moisture. The cut is often sliced thin, then dried or smoked at low temperature to make jerky.
[0.061, 0.296, 0.633] Agent refined response: A round roast is a cut of beef from the hindquarters, "eye" of the round is the section of the animal from the "eye" muscle (the muscle in the head), including the eye itself, and is a lean cut of meat. When cooked, it is tender and flavorful with a roast-like texture. Eye of round is a lesser-quality cut of beef and is often discarded. It is sometimes ground or used in stews or slow cooking. When roasted, it is more tender and flavorful than ground beef. It is not a popular cut of meat and is often less expensive.

Prompt: where do bullets go when fired in the air
Ground truth: Bullets fired into the air usually fall back with terminal velocities much lower than their muzzle velocity when they leave the barrel of a firearm. Nevertheless, people can be injured, sometimes fatally, when bullets discharged into the air fall back down to the ground. Bullets fired at angles less than vertical are more dangerous as the bullet maintains its angular ballistic trajectory and is far less likely to engage in tumbling motion; it therefore travels at speeds much higher than a bullet in free fall.
[0.000, 0.214, 0.620] Agent refined response: Bullets usually fall back to the ground unless they are spinning at the right angle to be captured by the Earth rotation so that they can be fired again. When fired at a high velocity, the bullet can penetrate the atmosphere, it will lose its forward motion, and its trajectory will be affected by the air resistance it encounters. As the bullet is heavier than air it will be subject to a downward motion and the air resistance will cause it to spin and lose its forward motion. It is impossible to predict the exact path of a bullet fired in the air as its trajectory is influenced by many factors and varies greatly depending upon the projectile and the environment.

Prompt: when was the national flag of india adopted
Ground truth: The National Flag of India is a horizontal rectangular tricolour of India saffron, white and India green; with the Ashoka Chakra, a 24-spoke wheel, in navy blue at its centre. It was adopted in its present form during a meeting of the Constituent Assembly held on 22 July 1947, and it became the official flag of the Dominion of India on 15 August 1947. The flag was subsequently retained as that of the Republic of India. In India, the term "tricolour" (Hindi: तिरंगा, translit. Tiraṅgā) almost always refers to the Indian national flag. The flag is based on the Swaraj flag, a flag of the Indian National Congress designed by Pingali Venkayya.
[0.054, 0.252, 0.603] Agent refined response: Adopted, predominantly red and saffron, and green flag and national emblem. A flag committee formed by the Indian National Congress met on 22 August 1928. The committee decided that the flag should be a tricolor and that the saffron color should be the first color on the flag. The committee adopted the flag, which was later approved by the Indian National Congress on 22 August 1888. The flag, whose design was inspired by the Swastika, was first hoisted on 22 August 128, which was declared a national holiday by the Indian National Congress.

Prompt: who is often associated with printing the first book using moveable type in germany
Ground truth: Johannes Gutenberg of Mainz, Germany is acknowledged as the first to invent a metal movable-type printing system in Europe, the printing press. Gutenberg was a goldsmith familiar with techniques of cutting punches for making coins from moulds. Between 1436 and 1450 he developed hardware and techniques for casting letters from matrices using a device called the hand mould. Gutenberg's key invention and contribution to movable-type printing in Europe, the hand mould, was the first practical means of making cheap copies of letterpunches in the vast quantities needed to print complete books, making the movable-type printing process a viable enterprise.
[0.000, 0.217, 0.602] Agent refined response: To some, the first book printed with moveable type was a luxury manuscript, the Ars minor. More widely accepted was a book printed by Johannes Gutenberg, a German goldsmith and printer. He developed a new technology, based on a screw press and metal moveable type, and created the first printed book--the Gutenberg Bible. Gutenberg was inspired by the bookmaking technology of the Chinese and had a printing press with moveable type; the first printing press with a movable type was a hand press, and the first printed book was a handbook on a metal craft called the Ars minor.

Prompt: when did the right to buy scheme start
Ground truth: After Margaret Thatcher became Prime Minister in May 1979, the legislation to implement the Right to Buy was passed in the Housing Act 1980. Michael Heseltine, in his role as Secretary of State for the Environment, was in charge of implementing the legislation. Some 6,000,000 people were affected; about one in three actually purchased their housing unit. Heseltine noted that "no single piece of legislation has enabled the transfer of so much capital wealth from the state to the people". He said the right to buy had two main objectives: to give people what they wanted, and to reverse the trend of ever-increasing dominance of the state over the life of the individual.
[0.000, 0.190, 0.500] Agent refined response: From "Section 1, to encourage greater transparency in pricing it is mandatory that the "Right to Buy" scheme should be offered to tenants in all local authorities, and to eligible tenants in the private sector, on the same terms as to those in the public sector. To provide a long-term solution to the problem of housing shortages, the government introduced a statutory Right to Buy scheme in 1981. While it was initially available only to local authority tenants, it was extended to tenants of private registered providers (such as housing associations) in 2012.

Prompt: where does the saying keeping up with the jones come from
Ground truth: The phrase originates with the comic strip Keeping Up with the Joneses, created by Arthur R. "Pop" Momand in 1913. The strip ran until 1940 in The New York World and various other newspapers. The strip depicts the social climbing McGinis family, who struggle to "keep up" with their neighbors, the Joneses of the title. The Joneses were unseen characters throughout the strip's run, often spoken of but never shown. The idiom keeping up with the Joneses has remained popular long after the strip's end.
[0.077, 0.305, 0.596] Agent refined response: The "Joneses" is allegedly derived from the Joneses of London. The phrase entered popular culture in the late 19th century (with attestations in the 1870s) in a context where the Joneses represented the middle class. The phrase also appeared in the 1880s in the United States, with the Joneses being the model of the "ideal American family". The phrase "keeping up with the Joneses" originally referred to the anxiety of those in the lower classes who wanted to emulate the perceived higher status of the Joneses. The phrase evolved into the modern idiom "keeping up with the Jones" in the mid-20th century.

Prompt: locations for the film an englishman who went up a hill
Ground truth: The film is based on a story heard by Christopher Monger from his grandfather about the real village of Taff's Well, in the old county of Glamorgan, and its neighbouring Garth Hill. Due to 20th century urbanisation of the area, it was filmed in the more rural Llanrhaeadr-ym-Mochnant and Llansilin in Powys. The Welsh Male Voice Choir used to provide background music throughout the film was, in fact, the London-based Gwalia Male Choir.
[0.000, 0.158, 0.483] Agent refined response: The producers claimed they could not remember where the hill came from-but a good guess would be the north Yorkshire moors, where the cameras were being filmed, which provided the film's backdrop even though the story was set in the Peak District of Derbyshire. Ian Quinlan a location manager told the Sheffield Telegraph: Peter Medak wanted a landscape that was rough and bleak but not too bleak because we didn't want to alienate the audience. We looked at several locations in North Yorkshire and decided on the moors because they offered a combination of ruggedness and accessibility."

Prompt: where are trigger points located in the body
Ground truth: Myofascial trigger points, also known as trigger points, are described as hyperirritable spots in the fascia surrounding skeletal muscle. They are associated with palpable nodules in taut bands of muscle fibers. They are a topic of ongoing controversy, as there is limited data to inform a scientific understanding of the phenomenon. Accordingly, a formal acceptance of myofascial "knots" as an identifiable source of pain is more common among bodyworkers, physical therapists, chiropractors, and osteopathic practitioners. Nonetheless, the concept of trigger points provides a framework which may be used to help address certain musculoskeletal pain.
[0.000, 0.150, 0.567] Agent refined response: Trigger points or myofascial trigger points refer to areas of taut or knotted muscle tissue. These also cause muscle spasms, pain, or limited range of motion. Trigger points also affect the muscles, ligaments, tendons, and nerves, as well as the fascia, which is the connective tissue that surrounds muscles, organs, and blood vessels. Trigger points also affect the nerves. Trigger points can also affect the nervous system. Trigger points can also affect blood flow and oxygenation. Trigger points can also affect other muscles and structures in the body. Trigger points can also affect movement and posture. Trigger points can also affect overall health. Trigger points can also affect quality of life. Trigger points can also affect pain and function. Trigger points can also affect muscle imbalances. Trigger points can also affect poor posture.

Prompt: ambulatory is a term used in health care to describe
Ground truth: Ambulatory care or outpatient care is medical care provided on an outpatient basis, including diagnosis, observation, consultation, treatment, intervention, and rehabilitation services. This care can include advanced medical technology and procedures even when provided outside of hospitals.
[0.000, 0.145, 0.614] Agent refined response: ambulatory care (such as clinics or doctors offices) are visited for the purpose of medical treatment, as opposed to the more formal hospital setting. Most of the time, patients do not have to stay overnight in the hospital for extended periods of time. In the case of some medical emergencies, ambulatory care may include transportation of patients in ambulances. Some examples of ambulatory care include routine physicals, pre-operative care, and outpatient therapy. Many medical specialties, including general practice, pediatrics, cardiology, neurology, and oncology, provide ambulatory care.

Ground truth: Egypt under the Muhammad Ali dynasty remained nominally an Ottoman province. It was granted the status of an autonomous vassal state or Khedivate in 1867. Isma'il Pasha and Tewfik Pasha governed Egypt as a quasi-independent state under Ottoman suzerainty until the British occupation of 1882. Nevertheless, the Khedivate of Egypt (1867--1914) remained a de jure Ottoman province until 5 November 1914, when it was declared a British protectorate in reaction to the decision of the Young Turks of the Ottoman Empire to join the First World War on the side of the Central Powers.

Prompt: what role does the president play in foreign affairs
Ground truth: Subject to the advice and consent role of the U.S. Senate, the President of the United States negotiates treaties with foreign nations, but treaties enter into force if ratified by two-thirds of the Senate. The President is also Commander in Chief of the United States Armed Forces, and as such has broad authority over the armed forces. Both the Secretary of State and ambassadors are appointed by the President, with the advice and consent of the Senate. The United States Secretary of State acts similarly to a foreign minister and under Executive leadership is the primary conductor of state-to-state diplomacy.
[0.145, 0.324, 0.638] Agent refined response: The President, with the Vice President and a Cabinet of executive departments, forms the executive branch of the federal government. The President serves as the Commander in Chief of the armed forces and is the chief architect of the nation’s foreign policy. The President’s role in foreign affairs is to: (1) Conduct diplomacy with foreign governments, and (2) Conduct military operations with foreign and domestic forces. The President has the power to negotiate and sign treaties, but must have the advice and consent of the Senate for ratification. The President has the power to recognize foreign governments, and has the authority to appoint ambassadors, other public officials, and military officers.

Prompt: where does the term pop music come from
Ground truth: According to the website of The New Grove Dictionary of Music and Musicians, called Grove Music Online, the term "pop music" "originated in Britain in the mid-1950s as a description for rock and roll and the new youth music styles that it influenced". The Oxford Dictionary of Music states that while pop's "earlier meaning meant concerts appealing to a wide audience. since the late 1950s, however, pop has had the special meaning of non-classical mus (ic), usually in the form of songs, performed by such artists as the Beatles, the Rolling Stones, ABBA, etc". Grove Music Online also states that ". in the early 1960s, (the term) ' pop music ' competed terminologically with beat music (in England), while in the USA its coverage overlapped (as it still does) with that of ' rock and roll '".
[0.000, 0.198, 0.566] Agent refined response: Pop has its roots in various forms of popular music, but has sometimes been used as a catch-all term for popular music, sometimes implying a lack of seriousness or artistic depth. The term has often been associated with "light" (rather than "serious") music, and has sometimes been used as a synonym for "popular music". In the 1950s, some musicologists described the style as "light" music, contrasting it with what they described as "serious" or "art" music, while others described the style as "popular music", contrasting it with what they described, in the 1970s, as "rock music".

Prompt: is the right to life liberty and the pursuit of happiness in the constitution
Ground truth: The phrase can also be found in Chapter III, Article 13 of the 1947 Constitution of Japan, and in President Ho Chi Minh's 1945 declaration of independence of the Democratic Republic of Vietnam. An alternative phrase "life, liberty, and property", is found in the Declaration of Colonial Rights, a resolution of the First Continental Congress. The Fifth Amendment and Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution declare that governments can not deprive any person of "life, liberty, or property" without due process of law. The oldest true horse ever found is a 45 million year old (Eocene) specimen of Eohippus. Prompt: sending money home to the native country is an example of
Ground truth: A remittance is a transfer of money by a foreign worker to an individual in his or her home country. Money sent home by migrants competes with international aid as one of the largest financial inflows to developing countries. Workers' remittances are a significant part of international capital flows, especially with regard to labour-exporting countries. In 2014, $436 billion went to developing countries, setting a new record. Overall global remittances totaled $582 billion in 2015. Some countries, such as India and China, receive tens of billions of US dollars in remittances each year from their expatriates and diaspora. In 2014, India received an estimated $70 billion and China an estimated $64 billion.

In 2015, India received more remittances than any other country in the world, with an inflow of $69 billion.

Prompt: what is foreign account tax compliance act (fatca)
Ground truth: The Foreign Account Tax Compliance Act (FATCA) is a 2010 United States federal law requiring all non-U.S. (' foreign ') financial institutions (FFIs) to search their records for customers with indicia of ' U.S.-person ' status, such as a U.S. place of birth, and to report the assets and identities of such persons to the U.S. Department of the Treasury. FATCA also requires such persons to self-report their non-U.S. financial assets annually to the Internal Revenue Service (IRS) on form 8938, which is in addition to the older and further redundant requirement to self-report them annually to the Financial Crimes Enforcement Network (FinCEN) on form 114 (also known as' FBAR '). Like U.S. income tax law, FATCA applies to U.S. residents and also to U.S. citizens and green card holders residing in other countries.
[0.137, 0.336, 0.685] Agent refined response: By the 11th of March 2010, the U.S. government enacted the Foreign Account Tax Compliance Act (FATCA). FATCA is a U.S. law requiring foreign financial institutions (FFIs) to report to the U.S. Internal Revenue Service (IRS) information on financial accounts held by U.S. taxpayers, to help the IRS detect and prevent tax evasion by U.S. taxpaying non-resident aliens (NRAs) of U.S. taxes on their worldwide income. The term also refers to the U.S. withholding tax (WHT) on certain U.S. source income paid to non-U.S. persons.

Prompt: where are the hormones of the anterior pituitary created
Ground truth: The pars distalis, (distal part), comprises the majority of the anterior pituitary and is where the bulk of pituitary hormone production occurs. The pars distalis contains two types of cells including chromophobe cells and chromophil cells. The chromophils can be further divided into acidophils (alpha cells) and basophils (beta cells). These cells all together produce hormones of the anterior pituitary, and release them into the blood stream.
[0.047, 0.250, 0.582] Agent refined response: Humans, with the exception of the pineal gland, have a monophasic anterior pituitary gland. The anterior pituitary gland secretes hormones from the hypothalamus (hypophysis), with the hypothalamus containing the neurohypophysis (vasopressin and oxytocin), the pars intermedia (prolactin) and the pars anterior (TSH, FSH, LH and ACTH). These hormones have a variety of functions, including regulation of metabolism, reproductive cycle, growth and development, and the immune system. The hypothalamus has two main parts, the ventromedial nucleus (VMN) and the arcuate nucleus (ARC), which produce the hormones of the anterior lobe (TSH, FSH, and LH) which regulate the thyroid, gonads and adrenal glands.

Prompt: to whom do the powers not given to the federal government by the constitution go
Ground truth: The Tenth Amendment (Amendment X) to the United States Constitution, which is part of the Bill of Rights, was ratified on December 15, 1791. It expresses the principle of federalism and states' rights, which strictly supports the entire plan of the original Constitution for the United States of America, by stating that the federal government possesses only those powers delegated to it by the United States Constitution. All remaining powers are reserved for the states or the people.
[0.091, 0.246, 0.633] Agent refined response: By the U.S. Constitution it is mandated that the powers not delegated to the United States by the Constitution, nor prohibited to it by the States, are reserved to the States respectively or to the people. This principle was enunciated by the U.S. Supreme Court in the 1803 case of Marbury v. Madison and by it the Court established a broad implication of the doctrine of States rights, as well as a limitation of the power of the federal government. This doctrine had been anticipated by the U.S. Constitution itself, by its provisions for a system of federal government, as well as by its republican character.

Ground truth: Eagle Scout is the highest rank one can receive in BSA. A Scout who attains this rank is called an Eagle Scout. Since its introduction in 1911, the Eagle Scout rank has been earned by more than two million young men. Requirements include earning at least 21 merit badges and demonstrating Scout Spirit through the Boy Scout Oath and Law, service, and leadership all before or by age 18. This includes an extensive service project that the Scout plans, organizes, leads, and manages. Eagle Scouts are presented with a medal and a badge that visibly recognizes the accomplishments of the Scout. Additional recognition can be earned through Eagle Palms, awarded for completing additional tenure, leadership, and merit badge requirements. Many famous Americans are Eagle Scouts: astronaut Neil Armstrong, film director Michael Moore, TV host Mike Rowe, Steven Spielberg, Mayor Michael Bloomberg, Secretary of Defense Robert Gates, and President Gerald Ford are just a small sample of Eagle Scouts.

Prompt: what is the full scientific name for the e. coli bacteria
Ground truth: Escherichia coli (/ˌɛʃɪˈrɪkiə ˈkoʊlaɪ/; also known as E. coli) is a Gram-negative, facultatively anaerobic, rod-shaped, coliform bacterium of the genus Escherichia that is commonly found in the lower intestine of warm-blooded organisms (endotherms). Most E. coli strains are harmless, but some serotypes can cause serious food poisoning in their hosts, and are occasionally responsible for product recalls due to food contamination. The harmless strains are part of the normal flora of the gut, and can benefit their hosts by producing vitamin K, and preventing colonization of the intestine with pathogenic bacteria, having a symbiotic relationship. E. coli is expelled into the environment within fecal matter. The bacterium grows massively in fresh fecal matter under aerobic conditions for 3 days, but its numbers decline slowly afterwards.
[0.122, 0.295, 0.659] Agent refined response: Secondary bacterial cultures Escherichia coli also E. coli Latin Escherichia coli with E. coli abbreviation. Genus Escherichia and species Escherichia coli. Family Escherichiaceae (Bacteroidaceae) and order Bacteroidales (Bacteroidales). Escherichia coli is a gram-negative, rod-shaped bacterium that is commonly found in the lower intestine of warm-blooded animals, but is commonly found in feces. E. coli is a normal inhabitant of the gastrointestinal tract and is considered a normal flora. E. coli can cause urinary tract infections, but is more likely to cause gastrointestinal infections, and is also a cause of food poisoning.

Ground truth: A cricket bat is a specialised piece of equipment used by batsmen in the sport of cricket to hit the ball, typically consisting of a cane handle attached to a flat-fronted willow-wood blade. The length of the bat may be no more than 38 inches (965 mm) and the width no more than 4.25 inches (108 mm). Its use is first mentioned in 1624. Since 1979, a rule change stipulated that bats can only be made from wood.
[0.052, 0.220, 0.606] Agent refined response: Walter R. Taylor, in his 1957 book The Art of Cricket, recommends that the length of the bat (which is the length of the handle) be between 35.5 inches (90 cm), and the width of the blade (which is the width of the face of the bat) be between 4.75 inches (12.1 cm) and 5.5 inches (14.0 cm). He recommends the width of the blade be between 25 and 30 percent of the overall length. (The standard length of the handle is 37.5 inches (95 cm).)

[0.046, 0.230, 0.593] Agent refined response: The development of a systematic method of naming the elements represents a significant step in the history of chemistry. The most widely used system of element nomenclature today was developed in the 1860s by the Russian chemist Dmitri Mendeleev, who was its first proponent, but his work was not fully recognized until after his death. However, its roots go back to the 1820s when Danish chemist J.J. Berzelius (1779--1850) who is generally credited as being the first scientist to use chemical symbols for elements. Berzelius assigned his symbols to elements based on their atomic weight. Berzelius began his work in 1813 but did not publish his findings until 1829.

Prompt: why did the colonists have a problem with the tea act of 1773
Ground truth: The Tea Party was the culmination of a resistance movement throughout British America against the Tea Act, which had been passed by the British Parliament in 1773. Colonists objected to the Tea Act because they believed that it violated their rights as Englishmen to "No taxation without representation", that is, to be taxed only by their own elected representatives and not by a British parliament in which they were not represented. In addition, the well-connected East India Company had been granted competitive advantages over colonial tea importers, who resented the move and feared additional infringement on their business. Protesters had successfully prevented the unloading of taxed tea in three other colonies, but in Boston, embattled Royal Governor Thomas Hutchinson refused to allow the tea to be returned to Britain.
[0.057, 0.204, 0.620] Agent refined response: In 1773 the British Parliament passed the Tea Act because it gave the British East India Company a monopoly on all tea in the American colonies, which was not only a tax but also a trade restriction, and did not allow other importers to sell tea in the colonies. The colonists, who had previously been accustomed to buying tea smuggled in from other ports, were resistant to paying taxes to the British government, and saw the Tea Act as yet another example of taxation without representation.

Prompt: how long did it take to build the great pyramid of egypt
Ground truth: Egyptologists believe the pyramid was built as a tomb for the Fourth Dynasty Egyptian pharaoh Khufu (often Hellenized as "Cheops") and was constructed over a 20-year period. Khufu's vizier, Hemiunu (also called Hemon) is believed by some to be the architect of the Great Pyramid. It is thought that, at construction, the Great Pyramid was originally 280 Egyptian Royal cubits tall (146.5 metres (480.6 ft)), but with erosion and absence of its pyramidion, its present height is 138.8 metres (455.4 ft). Each base side was 440 cubits, 230.4 metres (755.9 ft) long. The mass of the pyramid is estimated at 5.9 million tonnes. The volume, including an internal hillock, is roughly 2,500,000 cubic metres (88,000,000 cu ft).

The pyramid used 2.3 million blocks of stone, with each block weighing 2.5 to 50 tons.

Prompt: where do the secretory cells of endocrine glands secrete their products
Ground truth: Exocrine glands are glands that produce and secrete substances onto an epithelial surface by way of a duct. Examples of exocrine glands include sweat, salivary, mammary, ceruminous, lacrimal, sebaceous, and mucous. Exocrine glands are one of two types of glands in the human body, the other being endocrine glands, which secrete their products directly into the bloodstream. The liver and pancreas are both exocrine and endocrine glands; they are exocrine glands because they secrete products--bile and pancreatic juice--into the gastrointestinal tract through a series of ducts, and endocrine because they secrete other substances directly into the bloodstream.
[0.065, 0.227, 0.555] Agent refined response: Parathyroid glands--parathyroid glands secrete parathyroid hormone, a hormone that regulates calcium levels in the blood. Parathyroid glands are located in the neck. Parathyroid glands contain 20--40 follicles. Each follicle contains 10--100 parathyroid cells. The secretory cells of the parathyroid glands secrete their products into the bloodstream. The hormone (parathyroid hormone) acts to regulate calcium levels in the blood, bone, and soft tissues. Parathyroid hormone stimulates the release of calcium ions from bone. Parathyroid hormones stimulate the release of calcium ions into the blood. Parathyroid hormoness stimulate the release of the mineral phosphorus.

Ground truth: The Beer Hall Putsch, also known as the Munich Putsch, and, in German, as the Hitlerputsch, Hitler-Ludendorff-Putsch, Bürgerbräu-Putsch or mostly Marsch auf die Feldherrnhalle, was a failed coup attempt by the Nazi Party (NSDAP) leader Adolf Hitler--along with Generalquartiermeister Erich Ludendorff and other Kampfbund leaders--to seize power in Munich, Bavaria, during 8--9 November 1923. About two thousand Nazis marched to the centre of Munich, where they confronted the police, which resulted in the death of 16 Nazis and four police officers. Hitler was not wounded during the clash, although he locked his left arm with the right arm of Max Erwin von Scheubner-Richter who, when he was shot and killed, pulled Hitler to the pavement with him. Hitler escaped immediate arrest and was spirited off to safety in the countryside. After two days, Hitler was arrested and charged with treason.

Prompt: who said beware of the ides of march
Ground truth: In modern times, the Ides of March is best known as the date on which Julius Caesar was assassinated in 44 BC. Caesar was stabbed to death at a meeting of the Senate. As many as 60 conspirators, led by Brutus and Cassius, were involved. According to Plutarch, a seer had warned that harm would come to Caesar no later than the Ides of March. On his way to the Theatre of Pompey, where he would be assassinated, Caesar passed the seer and joked, "The Ides of March are come", implying that the prophecy had not been fulfilled, to which the seer replied "Aye, Caesar; but not gone." This meeting is famously dramatised in William Shakespeare's play Julius Caesar, when Caesar is warned by the soothsayer to "beware the Ides of March." The Roman biographer Suetonius identifies the "seer" as a haruspex named Spurinna.
[0.096, 0.245, 0.601] Agent refined response: No later than March 15 would be the time to beware of the Ides of March. This phrase has become a famous historical quote, and the day is widely known as "March 15". This is the origin of the phrase "Beware of the Ides of March". The phrase is a warning that Caesar had given to Brutus, which he used as a way of saying "be careful of what you wish for".

Columbus visited several other islands in the Bahamas before sailing to present-day Cuba and afterwards to Hispaniola.

Androgenetic alopecia (AGA) is a genetic disorder in which the body produces androgens (male hormones) that cause hair follicles to shrink and stop producing hair. Prompt: when does a dieback or population crash occur
Ground truth: In population dynamics and population ecology, overshoot occurs when a population temporarily exceeds the long term carrying capacity of its environment. The environment usually has mechanisms in place to prevent overshoot. For example, plants are only able to regenerate and regrow a few times after being consumed before completely dying off. The consequence of overshoot is called a collapse, a crash or a die-off in which there is a decline in population density. The entire sequence or trajectory undergone by the population and its environment together is often termed ' overshoot-and-collapse '.
[0.000, 0.178, 0.561] Agent refined response: The timing of diebacks can vary. The causes may be immediate or delayed. A dieback may occur in a single year or over several years. A dieback is usually only noticeable after its onset and is not always predictable. There are several factors that can cause a dieback, such as extreme weather events or the lack of water. The rate of dieback is determined by the severity of the drought and the individual plant's ability to survive. A dieback can occur in any plant and can be caused by a variety of factors. The plant's ability to survive is affected by its genetic makeup and its environment.

Prompt: what are the 3 layers of the meninges
Ground truth: The meninges (/məˈnɪndʒiːz/, singular: meninx (/ˈmiːnɪŋks/or/ˈmɛnɪŋks/), from Ancient Greek: μῆνιγξ, translit. mēninx, lit. ' membrane ', adjectival: meningeal/məˈnɪndʒəl/) are the three membranes that envelop the brain and spinal cord. In mammals, the meninges are the dura mater, the arachnoid mater, and the pia mater. Cerebrospinal fluid is located in the subarachnoid space between the arachnoid mater and the pia mater. The primary function of the meninges is to protect the central nervous system.
[0.138, 0.337, 0.609] Agent refined response: The meninges are the protective membranes of the central nervous system (CNS). The meninges cover the brain and spinal cord. Meningitis is inflammation of the meninges. The meninges are composed of the dura mater, arachnoid mater, and pia mater. The dura mater is the outermost meningeal layer (also called the tough, fibrous meningeal layer). The arachnoid mater is the middle meningeal layer (also called arachnoid membrane). The pia mater is the innermost meningeal layer (also called pial membrane). The pia mater is also called the pial membrane. The pia mater is a thin, delicate membrane of the brain, adhering closely to the surface of the brain. The arachnoid mater is a thin, vascular membrane of the brain.

Prompt: what states do not allow daylight savings time
Ground truth: Daylight saving time in the United States is the practice of setting the clock forward by one hour during the warmer part of the year, so that evenings have more daylight and mornings have less. Most areas of the United States observe daylight saving time (DST), the exceptions being Arizona (except for the Navajo, who do observe daylight saving time on tribal lands), Hawaii, and the overseas territories of American Samoa, Guam, the Northern Mariana Islands, Puerto Rico, and the United States Virgin Islands. The Uniform Time Act of 1966 established the system of uniform Daylight Saving Time throughout the US.
[0.283, 0.415, 0.682] Agent refined response: Most of the United States observe daylight saving time. However, the states of Hawaii (except for the islands of Kauai, Hawaii, and Maui), most of Arizona, and the territories of the U.S. Virgin Islands, American Samoa, Guam, the Northern Mariana Islands, Puerto Rico, and the U.S. Virgin Islands are the only states not on daylight saving time. The first and last Sunday in the month of March and the first Sunday in November of each year mark the beginning and end of daylight saving time.

Prompt: where are the cones in the eye located
Ground truth: Cone cells, or cones, are one of three types of photoreceptor cells in the retina of mammalian eyes (e.g. the human eye). They are responsible for color vision and function best in relatively bright light, as opposed to rod cells, which work better in dim light. Cone cells are densely packed in the fovea centralis, a 0.3 mm diameter rod-free area with very thin, densely packed cones which quickly reduce in number towards the periphery of the retina. There are about six to seven million cones in a human eye and are most concentrated towards the macula. The commonly cited figure of six million cone cells in the human eye was found by Osterberg in 1935. Oyster's textbook (1999) cites work by Curcio et al. (1990) indicating an average close to 4.5 million cone cells and 90 million rod cells in the human retina.
[0.000, 0.197, 0.598] Agent refined response: Cones, like most other photoreceptors, are concentrated in the central retina, where visual acuity is highest, but are fewer in the periphery. The distribution of cones varies by species, with the highest density in the fovea, where the cones are arranged in a mosaic. They are most numerous at the center of the retina, where they are densely packed. They are typically found at the back of the eye, opposite the lens. The cones are most numerous at the center of vision and are arranged in a mosaic pattern. The central 10 degrees of the visual field is where most of the cones are located.

Prompt: where is connective tissue found in the body
Ground truth: Connective tissue (CT) is one of the four basic types of animal tissue, along with epithelial tissue, muscle tissue, and nervous tissue. It develops from the mesoderm. Connective tissue is found in between other tissues everywhere in the body, including the nervous system. In the central nervous system, the three outer membranes (the meninges) that envelop the brain and spinal cord are composed of connective tissue. They support and protect the body. All connective tissue consists of three main components: fibers (elastic and collagenous fibers), ground substance and cells. Not all authorities include blood or lymph as connective tissue because they lack the fiber component. All are immersed in the body water.

The meninges contain nerve fibers and produce cerebrospinal fluid. They include blood vessels and nerve fibers. The meninges help protect the brain and spinal cord and facilitate their growth.

Prompt: when does april fools day end at noon
Ground truth: In Poland, prima aprilis ("1 April" in Latin) as a day of jokes is a centuries-long tradition. It is a day in which many jokes are told; various hoaxes--sometimes very sophisticated--are prepared by people, media (which often cooperate to make the "information" more credible) and even public institutions. Serious activities are usually avoided, and generally every word said on April 1st can be a lie, or a joke. The conviction for this is so strong that the Polish anti-Turkish alliance with Leopold I signed on April 1, 1683, was backdated to March 31. However, for some in Poland prima aprilis ends at noon of April 1st, and prima aprilis jokes after that hour are considered inappropriate and not classy.

In the United Kingdom (and other Commonwealth countries) the day is usually celebrated on Saturday (and sometimes Friday) 31 March, although in the past, other days have been used. The day usually ends at noon, although the exact time is not usually specified. The last day of the celebration is usually not mentioned in the sources, although some claim the celebration is usually done on the next day.

Prompt: what is the approximate volume of the brain
Ground truth: Studies on human brain size, largely based on participants of European ancestry, tend to find an average adult brain volume of 1260 cubic centimeters (cm) for men and 1130 cm for women. There is, however, substantial variation between individuals; one study of 46 adults, aged 22--49 years and of mainly European descent, found an average brain volume of 1273.6 cm for men, with a range of 1052.9 to 1498.5 cm, and 1131.1 cm for women, with a range of 974.9 to 1398.1 cm.
[0.056, 0.170, 0.579] Agent refined response: Human brain studies suggest that average brain size varies little across different populations. However, some studies have reported that the average human brain has grown by about 1.2 % over the past 10,000 years. The average human brain weighs approximately 1.3 kilograms (2.9 pounds), but is roughly spherical in shape. Studies have estimated that the human brain has a volume of 1,300 cubic centimeters (cm), with individual variation being greater than 10 % between people. In contrast, the average human skull is approximately 1,400 cm in volume, with the largest human skulls found in the fossil record having been those of the human ancestor Homo erectus.

Ground truth: The Santa Fe Trail was a 19th-century transportation route through central North America that connected Independence, Missouri with Santa Fe, New Mexico. Pioneered in 1821 by William Becknell, it served as a vital commercial highway until the introduction of the railroad to Santa Fe in 1880. Santa Fe was near the end of the El Camino Real de Tierra Adentro which carried trade from Mexico City.

The trail was heavily used in the 1840s to transport goods from the United States to Mexico, which was involved in the Mexican War of Independence. Prompt: the elements in each period have the same number of
Ground truth: A period in the periodic table is one of the horizontal rows, all of those elements have the same number of electron shells. Going across a period, each element has one more proton and is less metallic than its predecessor. Arranged this way, groups of elements in the same column have similar chemical and physical properties, reflecting the periodic law. For example, the alkali metals lie in the first column (group 1) and share similar properties, such as high reactivity and the tendency to lose one electron to arrive at a noble-gas electronic configuration. As of 2016 a total of 118 elements have been discovered and confirmed.

Prompt: when does the heart develop and begin pumping blood
Ground truth: Heart development refers to the prenatal development of the human heart. This begins with the formation of two endocardial tubes which merge to form the tubular heart, also called the primitive heart tube, that loops and septates into the four chambers and paired arterial trunks that form the adult heart. The heart is the first functional organ in vertebrate embryos, and in the human, beats spontaneously by week 4 of development.
[0.048, 0.237, 0.587] Agent refined response: Before birth (gestation), the heart initially forms from two tube -like structures, called the first and second heart tubes. This begins at approximately 2 weeks (14 days) of gestation with a fusion of the first and second heart tubes forming the primitive heart tube. The first heart tube forms from the forerunbs and the second from the hindbrain. The first heart tube forms the left ventricle and left atrium, and the second forms the right ventricle and right atrium. The right ventricle and right atria are derived from the third and fourth arches, and the left ventricle and left auricle, from the fourth and fifth arches.

Prompt: where does decussation occur in the spinothalamic pathway
Ground truth: The axons of the tract cells cross over (decussate) to the other side of the spinal cord via the anterior white commissure, and to the anterolateral corner of the spinal cord (hence the spinothalamic tract being part of the anterolateral system). Decussation usually occurs 1-2 spinal nerve segments above the point of entry. The axons travel up the length of the spinal cord into the brainstem, specifically the rostral ventromedial medulla.
[0.103, 0.343, 0.699] Agent refined response: Spinal cord neurons send axons to the contralateral (opposite) side of the body via the decussation of the posterior white commissure, the anterior white commissure and the anterior commissure of the medulla oblongata (the latter via the cranial nerves). Thus, the decussation of the posterior commissure results in the cross-over of the ascending spinothalamic tracts, while the decussation of the anterior commissure results in the cross over of the descending spinothalamic tract. Decussation typically occurs in the ventral white matter of the spinal cord. Decussation is the only area of cross-over in the spinal cord.

Prompt: what was the religion in the virginia colony
Ground truth: In the 1740s, the established Anglican church had about 70 parish priests around the colony. There was no bishop, and indeed, there was fierce political opposition to having a bishop in the colony. The Anglican priests were supervised directly by the Bishop of London. Each county court gave tax money to the local vestry, composed of prominent laymen. The vestry provided the priest a glebe of 200 or 300 acres (1.2 km), a house, and perhaps some livestock. The vestry paid him an annual salary of 16,000 pounds-of-tobacco, plus 20 shillings for every wedding and funeral. While not poor, the priests lived modestly and their opportunities for improvement were slim.

The church was also responsible for administering the oath of allegiance and enforcing the oath of abjuration of the English monarch (in 1677). In 1619, the Church of England had been established in the colony and had authority over the religious affairs of the colony (and the colonists).

Ground truth: In an attempt to bolster the estate's financial situation during the Great Depression, Cornelia and her husband opened Biltmore to the public in March 1930 at the request of the City of Asheville, which hoped the attraction would revitalize the area with tourism. Biltmore closed during World War II and in 1942, 62 paintings and 17 sculptures were moved to the estate by train from the National Gallery of Art in Washington, D.C. to protect them in the event of an attack on the United States. The Music Room on the first floor was never finished, so it was used for storage until 1944, when the possibility of an attack became more remote. Among the works stored were the Gilbert Stuart portrait of George Washington and works by Rembrandt, Raphael, and Anthony van Dyck. David Finley, the gallery director, was a friend of Edith Vanderbilt and had stayed at the estate.

During the Great Depression, the estate was closed to the public and was used as a temporary home for North Carolina State University's Agricultural Extension Service and the YMCA's Youth Villages. In 1942, the estate was taken over by the United States government to serve as a temporary military hospital during World War II.

Prompt: what do the 3 dots mean in math
Ground truth: In logical argument and mathematical proof, the therefore sign (∴) is generally used before a logical consequence, such as the conclusion of a syllogism. The symbol consists of three dots placed in an upright triangle and is read therefore. It is encoded at U + 2234 ∴ therefore (HTML & # 8756; &there4;). For common use in Microsoft Office hold the ALT key and type "8756". While it is not generally used in formal writing, it is used in mathematics and shorthand. It is complementary to U + 2235 ∵ because (HTML & # 8757;).
[0.000, 0.154, 0.530] Agent refined response: In addition to also appearing in computer programming, the three dots also appear in mathematics to signify different things, such as: therefore, but in more formal settings; in many mathematical contexts, the three dots are used to indicate that there are more results to come. For example:  a + 3 = 10; but there are infinitely many more numbers to add to this equation. This is because the equation does not specify the last number in the sequence (i.e. 10 is just a placeholder). This use of three dots (as in: add three more numbers) is a common way to indicate there is a large amount of information to come.

A decade of instability followed, marred by fractious coalition governments and frequent impositions of President's rule. The instability ended with 1977 election victory of the Communist Party of India (Marxist) (CPM). Headed by Jyoti Basu, the CPM-led Left Front government was in office for over 23 years (1977--2000). Left rule in West Bengal continued for another 10 years under Buddhadeb Bhattacharya, before its defeat in the 2011 election by the Trinamool Congress. Appointed on 20 May 2011, Trinamool leader Mamata Banerjee is the current incumbent, the state's first woman chief minister.

Prompt: what is the maximum data rate for the 802.11a standard select one
Ground truth: The 802.11 a standard uses the same data link layer protocol and frame format as the original standard, but an OFDM based air interface (physical layer). It operates in the 5 GHz band with a maximum net data rate of 54 Mbit/s, plus error correction code, which yields realistic net achievable throughput in the mid-20 Mbit/s.
[0.000, 0.167, 0.619] Agent refined response: In 802.11b / g / n and ac / ad / af, the maximum data rate has been 600 Mbps. At 5 GHz, it has been 1.2 Gbps. The 802.11ad standard uses the 60 GHz frequency band and can reach 7 Gbps (gigabit Ethernet). The 802.15.4 standard, which is used for Zigbee, can reach 20 Mbps (megabit Ethernet). The standard is limited to 20 Mbps (the same as the Zigbee standard).

Prompt: where does the term dog and pony show come from
Ground truth: The term was originally used in the United States in the late-19th and early-20th centuries to refer to small traveling circuses that toured through small towns and rural areas. The name derives from the common use of performing dogs and ponies as the main attractions of the events. Performances were generally held in open-air arenas, such as race tracks or public spaces in localities that were too small or remote to attract larger, more elaborate performers or performances. The most notorious was "Prof. Gentry's Famous Dog & Pony Show," started when teenager Henry Gentry and his brothers started touring in 1886 with their act, originally entitled "Gentry's Equine and Canine Paradox." It started small, but evolved into a full circus show. Other early dog and pony shows included Morris' Equine and Canine Paradoxes (1883) and Hurlburt's Dog and Pony Show (late 1880s).
[0.027, 0.132, 0.482] Agent refined response: From the 1920s to 1940s--this is a time when a person is running for a political office. This is a way to get people to vote for you. It is called "The Dog and Pony Show" because: * The dogs represent the incumbent. * The ponies represent the challengers. * The incumbent dogs are able to use all of the resources they have to keep the ponies from getting the nomination. * The ponies are all energetic and lively, with a lot of potential to win the race. * The dogs are all tired and worn out from the race. * The incumbent dogs have all the money and resources to keep the ponies from getting into the race. * The ponies are all tired and worn out from their efforts.

Prompt: who is recognized as the founder of islam
Ground truth: According to tradition, the Islamic prophet Muhammad was born in Mecca around the year 570. His family belonged to the Quraysh. When he was about forty years old, he began receiving what Muslims consider to be divine revelations delivered through the angel Gabriel, which would later form the Quran, enjoining him to proclaim a strict monotheistic faith, warn his compatriots of the impending Judgement Day, and castigate social injustices of his city. Muhammad's message won over a handful of followers and was met with increasing opposition from notables of Mecca. In 618, after he lost protection with the death of his influential uncle Abu Talib, Muhammad took flight to the city of Yathrib (subsequently called Medina) where he was joined by his followers. Later generations would count this event, known as the hijra, as the start of the Islamic era.
[0.000, 0.176, 0.519] Agent refined response: That was a huge success. There was a debate as to whether the Quran was the word of God or the word of Muhammad. This was also the beginning of the Islamic Golden Age. The Arabian Peninsula had been a crossroads of trade and religion since the ancient period. The Islamic conquests of the Sassanid Empire and the Byzantine Empire (also known as the Eastern Roman Empire) continued under the Rashidun Caliphate (632--661 CE), with the Islamic conquest of Persia (633--651 CE) and the Islamic conquest of Syria (634--669 CE) being considered the most significant. The conquests were also marked by the Islamic conquest of Egypt (641 CE) and the Islamic invasion of Anatolia (modern-day Turkey).

Prompt: where does us highway 1 start and end
Ground truth: U.S. Route 1 (US 1) is a major north--south U.S. Highway that serves the East Coast of the United States. It runs 2,369 miles (3,813 km), from Fort Kent, Maine, at the Canada--US border, south to Key West, Florida, making it the longest north--south road in the United States. US 1 is generally paralleled by Interstate 95 (I-95), though the former is significantly farther west (inland) between Jacksonville, Florida, and Petersburg, Virginia. The highway connects most of the major cities of the East Coast--including Miami, Jacksonville, Richmond, Washington, D.C., Baltimore, Philadelphia, New York City and Boston, passing from the Southeastern United States to New England.
[0.053, 0.239, 0.584] Agent refined response: US Highway 1, the Atlantic coastline of the United States from Key West Florida to Acadia National Park in Maine. While the eastern terminus of U.S. Route 1 is generally considered to be the border of Delaware, the western terminus is generally located in the city of Everett, Washington, with the mainline of US 1 ending at the intersection of US 99 and 10th Street NW, just south of downtown Everett. The US 1 corridor is a popular route for vacation travel, with many scenic coastal towns and roadside attractions along the way.

Prompt: first jnanpith award was an autor of which language
Ground truth: G. Sankara Kurup, (3 June 1901, Nayathode, Kingdom of Cochin (now in Ernakulam district, Kerala, India)--2 February 1978, Vappalassery, Angamaly, Ernakulam district, Kerala), better known as Mahakavi G (The Great Poet G), was the first winner of the Jnanpith Award, India's highest literary award. He won the prize in 1965 for his collection of poems in Malayalam Odakkuzhal (The Bamboo Flute, 1950). With part of the prize money he established the literary award Odakkuzhal in 1968. He was also the recipient of the Soviet Land Nehru Award, in 1967, and the Padma Bhushan in 1968. His poetry collection Viswadarshanam won the Kerala Sahitya Akademi Award in 1961 and Kendra Sahitya Akademi Award in 1963.
[0.074, 0.211, 0.570] Agent refined response: The Jnanpith Award, India's highest literary award, recognizes outstanding contributions in the Marathi language. It was established in 1965. Its recipients (1965 onwards) include M.T. Vasudevan Nair, U.R. Ananthamurthy, R.K. Narayan, and Mahasweta Devi. The Jnanpith Award was awarded to Dr. R.K. Narayan in 1981. His work in English, Malgudi Days, was also published in Marathi. He also wrote the Marathi plays, Chayavarga and Tumhaar Kavya. His Marathi novels, Swapnacharya and Natyacharya, were published in 1975 and 1981.

Prompt: what does it mean to be on parole
Ground truth: Parole is a temporary release of a prisoner who agrees to certain conditions before the completion of the maximum sentence period, originating from the French parole ("voice, spoken words"). The term became associated during the Middle Ages with the release of prisoners who gave their word.
[0.000, 0.180, 0.501] Agent refined response: That some or all of their sentences are reduced to time served, or probation or parole is possible for offenders to be eligible for. Offenders are not required to serve a minimum of time. However, probation and parole are not the same thing. Parole, however, is a type of supervision that may be imposed after the offender has served a portion of the sentence. Parole supervision, however, is not a form of punishment, but rather a form of supervision in the community. Parole officers are responsible for monitoring the offender in the community, reporting any violations of parole conditions to the Board of Parole, and making recommendations to the Board regarding whether or not the offender should be returned to prison.
