Prompt: the origins of the stations of the cross
Ground truth: The Stations of the Cross or the Way of the Cross, also known as the Way of Sorrows or the Via Crucis, refers to a series of images depicting Jesus Christ on the day of his crucifixion and accompanying prayers. The stations grew out of imitations of Via Dolorosa in Jerusalem which is believed to be the actual path Jesus walked to Mount Calvary. The object of the stations is to help the Christians faithful to make a spiritual pilgrimage through contemplation of the Passion of Christ. It has become one of the most popular devotions and the stations can be found in many Western Christian churches, including Anglican, Lutheran, Methodist, and Roman Catholic ones.
[0.107, 0.298, 0.572] Agent refined response: Of the 14 Stations of the Cross commemorating the Passion of Jesus Christ is the oldest of the devotions to Jesus in the Catholic Church although the first to be officially recognised is the Way of the Cross, as the Stations are not considered to be a separate devotion, although the stations are numbered in a standard order (the  order is not of any particular importance). The devotion to the Stations of the Cross is, however, the most widely spread and widely practised of all the devotions to Jesus, and is inseparably associated with the development of the Catholic Church.

Prompt: when does a limit of a function not exist
Ground truth: Formal definitions, first devised in the early 19th century, are given below. Informally, a function f assigns an output f (x) to every input x. We say the function has a limit L at an input p: this means f (x) gets closer and closer to L as x moves closer and closer to p. More specifically, when f is applied to any input sufficiently close to p, the output value is forced arbitrarily close to L. On the other hand, if some inputs very close to p are taken to outputs that stay a fixed distance apart, we say the limit does not exist.
[0.067, 0.168, 0.578] Agent refined response: As regards as an infinite series, a limit may not exist if the series does not converge, i.e. the series goes on indefinitely (i.e., an infinite number of terms). An example of a function that does not have a limit is f (x) =  1 / (1 + x), as the function can be made as large as one likes by taking x large enough (i.e., the function can be made as large (in absolute value) as one likes). The limit of the function as x approaches infinity is 0, but it does not exist. It is also possible to have a function (e.g. f (x) = x) where the limit of the function does not exist at a single point (i.e. the function does not have a limit at any point).

Prompt: what does the sun represent on the uruguay flag
Ground truth: The horizontal stripes on the flag represent the nine original departments of Uruguay, based on the U.S flag, where the stripes represent the original 13 colonies. The first flag designed in 1828 had 9 light blue stripes; this number was reduced to 4 in 1830 due to visibility problems from distance. The Sun of May represents the May Revolution of 1810; according to the historian Diego Abad de Santillán, the Sun of May is a figurative sun that represents Inti, the sun god of the Inca religion. It also appears in the Flag of Argentina and the Coat of Arms of Bolivia.

"De la luz del sol, de la gloria del aire" (Translated to English: Of the light of the sun, of the glory of the air).

Prompt: when did beryl markham fly across the atlantic
Ground truth: When Markham decided to take on the Atlantic crossing, no female pilot had yet flown non-stop from Europe to New York, and no woman had made the westward flight solo, though several had died trying. Markham hoped to claim both records. On 4 September 1936, she took off from Abingdon, England. After a 20-hour flight, her Vega Gull, The Messenger, suffered fuel starvation due to icing of the fuel tank vents, and she crash-landed at Baleine Cove on Cape Breton Island, Nova Scotia, Canada. She became the first person to make it from England to North America non-stop from east to west. She was celebrated as an aviation pioneer.
[0.071, 0.190, 0.614] Agent refined response: Because of a long-standing friendship with British aviator Amy Johnson, Markham wanted to break her solo flight record. On 26 January 1936, she took off from Darlington, flying via Cairo to Cape Town, where she was the first woman to fly solo across the continent. She then flew on to Johannesburg, where she made the first solo transatlantic flight by a woman (with no refueling). Markham finally landed in Darlington on 23 February 1936. The flight took 11 days, 20 hours and 56 minutes. She also made the first solo flight from London to Cape Town.

Prompt: where does eye of the round roast come from
Ground truth: A round steak is a beef steak from the "round", the rear leg of the cow. The round is divided into cuts including the eye (of) round, bottom round, and top round, with or without the "round" bone (femur), and may include the knuckle (sirloin tip), depending on how the round is separated from the loin. This is a lean cut and it is moderately tough. Lack of fat and marbling makes round dry out when cooked with dry-heat cooking methods like roasting or grilling. Round steak is commonly prepared with slow moist-heat methods including braising, to tenderize the meat and maintain moisture. The cut is often sliced thin, then dried or smoked at low temperature to make jerky.
[0.000, 0.293, 0.611] Agent refined response: A round roast is a cut of beef from the short loin, consisting of the muscle from the hip. The "eye" of the roast (sometimes spelled "eye-round" or eye of round) is a smaller, thicker cut from the rear of the roast. The name comes from the shape of the cut, resembling an eye. This cut is also known as a "butler steak" or "rump steak" in some countries, and is leaner than the more tender and flavorful "eye of the loin" cut.

[0.065, 0.205, 0.598] Agent refined response: Daylight saving time in Canada starts in most provinces on the second Sunday of March, 2018, or (NST) Newfoundland Standard Time (UTC-3: 30). Most provinces observe Daylight Saving Time except for Saskatchewan and most of Yukon. However, Saskatchewan (UTC-6) and most of Yukon (UTC-8) are exempt. The exception is a few First Nations reserves, which may observe Daylight Saving Time. There are also some areas of the Arctic which observe the Atlantic Time Zone, but they do not observe the daylight saving time. The official start and end dates of Daylight Saving Time are determined by the provinces.

Prompt: what was the alliance between great britian russia and france known as
Ground truth: The Triple Entente (from French entente (ɑ̃tɑ̃t) "friendship, understanding, agreement") refers to the understanding linking the Russian Empire, the French Third Republic, and the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland after the signing of the Anglo-Russian Entente on 31 August 1907. The understanding between the three powers, supplemented by agreements with Japan and Portugal, was a powerful counterweight to the Triple Alliance of Germany, Austria-Hungary, and Italy.
[0.000, 0.240, 0.567] Agent refined response: The alliance was called the Triple Entente. During World War I, it was formed with the assistance of Italy, although Italy was never fully committed to the alliance. Although the Triple Entente was defeated by the Central Powers in 1918, it was the main military alliance with Britain and France as the main parties. It was also the precursor to the post-war League of Nations and was the foundation of the modern European security order. Although the alliance was originally formed to counter the threat of Germany it was also the first time that Britain and France cooperated with Russia in military matters.

Prompt: where do bullets go when fired in the air
Ground truth: Bullets fired into the air usually fall back with terminal velocities much lower than their muzzle velocity when they leave the barrel of a firearm. Nevertheless, people can be injured, sometimes fatally, when bullets discharged into the air fall back down to the ground. Bullets fired at angles less than vertical are more dangerous as the bullet maintains its angular ballistic trajectory and is far less likely to engage in tumbling motion; it therefore travels at speeds much higher than a bullet in free fall.
[0.000, 0.172, 0.606] Agent refined response: Bullets usually lose most of their velocity when fired at sea level because the air resistance they encounter can only push them downward with a force equal to their weight. If the bullet is fired at a steep angle, it will penetrate the atmosphere at a higher velocity, before it begins to lose its velocity, so the bullet can travel long distances in the atmosphere and its trajectory is influenced by the air resistance and the angle of its trajectory. The bullet can be damaged by the friction with the air and its trajectory is influenced by its mass and shape. Bullets are designed to be aerodynamic and are made of materials that can withstand the heat generated by the friction with the air.

Prompt: who is often associated with printing the first book using moveable type in germany
Ground truth: Johannes Gutenberg of Mainz, Germany is acknowledged as the first to invent a metal movable-type printing system in Europe, the printing press. Gutenberg was a goldsmith familiar with techniques of cutting punches for making coins from moulds. Between 1436 and 1450 he developed hardware and techniques for casting letters from matrices using a device called the hand mould. Gutenberg's key invention and contribution to movable-type printing in Europe, the hand mould, was the first practical means of making cheap copies of letterpunches in the vast quantities needed to print complete books, making the movable-type printing process a viable enterprise.
[0.062, 0.220, 0.628] Agent refined response: To illustrate the type, a movable-type printing press was invented by Johannes Gutenberg, a German goldsmith. In the early 15th century, a number of printers were experimenting with moveable type; however, Gutenberg was the first to make it commercially viable. The Gutenberg Bible, a Protestant Bible, was the first book printed using moveable type and a printing press. It was published in 1455 and was a major factor in the spread of the Reformation and the Protestant movement.

Prompt: when was the national flag of india adopted
Ground truth: The National Flag of India is a horizontal rectangular tricolour of India saffron, white and India green; with the Ashoka Chakra, a 24-spoke wheel, in navy blue at its centre. It was adopted in its present form during a meeting of the Constituent Assembly held on 22 July 1947, and it became the official flag of the Dominion of India on 15 August 1947. The flag was subsequently retained as that of the Republic of India. In India, the term "tricolour" (Hindi: तिरंगा, translit. Tiraṅgā) almost always refers to the Indian national flag. The flag is based on the Swaraj flag, a flag of the Indian National Congress designed by Pingali Venkayya.
[0.107, 0.300, 0.588] Agent refined response: Adopted and flown, and all Indian states and territories on 22 August 1947. A flag was chosen by the Constituent Assembly of India on 22 July 1947; the flag that was chosen became the flag of India. The national flag was adopted and flown on 22 August 1883, when the first flag of India was hoisted by the Indian National Congress, on the banks of the Hooghly River, Kolkata. The flag, which was a red flag with the tricolor was first flown by the Indian National Congress, on 22 August 11, 1883, at the Indian National Congress session, held on the banks of the Hoogly River, Kolkata.

Prompt: when did the right to buy scheme start
Ground truth: After Margaret Thatcher became Prime Minister in May 1979, the legislation to implement the Right to Buy was passed in the Housing Act 1980. Michael Heseltine, in his role as Secretary of State for the Environment, was in charge of implementing the legislation. Some 6,000,000 people were affected; about one in three actually purchased their housing unit. Heseltine noted that "no single piece of legislation has enabled the transfer of so much capital wealth from the state to the people". He said the right to buy had two main objectives: to give people what they wanted, and to reverse the trend of ever-increasing dominance of the state over the life of the individual.
[0.000, 0.149, 0.485] Agent refined response: From "Nationalised industries, privatisation", it states that "Privatisation began in 1981 under the Conservative government to reduce public spending on "uneconomic" industries and to introduce "competition" and "efficiency" into the sector" and "In 1988 the first major sale of a nationalised industry took place when the government sold off 20 per cent of British Gas". So, while exact dates vary (some major sales took place in 1988), one can say that the start of some form of sale (i.e. 20 per cent) occurred in 1988.

The strip depicts the social climbing McGinis family, who struggle to "keep up" with their neighbors, the Joneses of the title. The Joneses were unseen characters throughout the strip's run, often spoken of but never shown. The idiom keeping up with the Joneses has remained popular long after the strip's end.

Ground truth: Ambulatory care or outpatient care is medical care provided on an outpatient basis, including diagnosis, observation, consultation, treatment, intervention, and rehabilitation services. This care can include advanced medical technology and procedures even when provided outside of hospitals.
[0.053, 0.234, 0.706] Agent refined response: Outpatient (ambulatory) care (including office visits) are where patients are treated for medical conditions, and the majority of care is delivered on an outpatient basis. Some examples of ambulatory care include routine check-ups, physicals, and most surgical procedures. Most of these services can be delivered in outpatient settings such as clinics, doctor offices, and urgent care centers. Ambulatory care can include an array of services including surgery, chemotherapy, radiation therapy, imaging, laboratory testing, and rehabilitation. In some cases, some of these services may be provided in a hospital outpatient department.

Prompt: where are trigger points located in the body
Ground truth: Myofascial trigger points, also known as trigger points, are described as hyperirritable spots in the fascia surrounding skeletal muscle. They are associated with palpable nodules in taut bands of muscle fibers. They are a topic of ongoing controversy, as there is limited data to inform a scientific understanding of the phenomenon. Accordingly, a formal acceptance of myofascial "knots" as an identifiable source of pain is more common among bodyworkers, physical therapists, chiropractors, and osteopathic practitioners. Nonetheless, the concept of trigger points provides a framework which may be used to help address certain musculoskeletal pain.
[0.053, 0.230, 0.589] Agent refined response: Trigger points or painful spots. also known as "tender points" or "Trigger points", are areas of taut muscle tissue that also contain a "spindle" of muscle fibers. These spindles are also known as "myofascial trigger points" and can cause pain, stiffness, numbness, or weakness in muscles and connective tissue. Other trigger points may be located along nerves or their branches (peripheral nerves) or within the spinal cord and brain (central nervous system). Trigger points can cause pain that radiates to other areas of the body and may be associated with muscle spasms and limited range of motion.

Prompt: what role does the president play in foreign affairs
Ground truth: Subject to the advice and consent role of the U.S. Senate, the President of the United States negotiates treaties with foreign nations, but treaties enter into force if ratified by two-thirds of the Senate. The President is also Commander in Chief of the United States Armed Forces, and as such has broad authority over the armed forces. Both the Secretary of State and ambassadors are appointed by the President, with the advice and consent of the Senate. The United States Secretary of State acts similarly to a foreign minister and under Executive leadership is the primary conductor of state-to-state diplomacy.
[0.110, 0.282, 0.656] Agent refined response: The President, with the Vice President and a Cabinet of executive departments, exercises broad executive power over the nation. The President serves as Commander-in-Chief of the U.S. Armed Forces (Article II, Section 2 of the U.S. Constitution). The President appoints officials to head the 15 executive departments, independent agencies, and other executive agencies that are nominated by the President and confirmed by the Senate. The President has the power to negotiate treaties, but must have them ratified by a two-thirds vote in the Senate to take effect. The President has the power to grant pardons and reprieves.

Prompt: who was the ottoman governor who led egypt in the years following the napoleonic wars
Ground truth: Egypt under the Muhammad Ali dynasty remained nominally an Ottoman province. It was granted the status of an autonomous vassal state or Khedivate in 1867. Isma'il Pasha and Tewfik Pasha governed Egypt as a quasi-independent state under Ottoman suzerainty until the British occupation of 1882. Nevertheless, the Khedivate of Egypt (1867--1914) remained a de jure Ottoman province until 5 November 1914, when it was declared a British protectorate in reaction to the decision of the Young Turks of the Ottoman Empire to join the First World War on the side of the Central Powers.

Prompt: where does the term pop music come from
Ground truth: According to the website of The New Grove Dictionary of Music and Musicians, called Grove Music Online, the term "pop music" "originated in Britain in the mid-1950s as a description for rock and roll and the new youth music styles that it influenced". The Oxford Dictionary of Music states that while pop's "earlier meaning meant concerts appealing to a wide audience. since the late 1950s, however, pop has had the special meaning of non-classical mus (ic), usually in the form of songs, performed by such artists as the Beatles, the Rolling Stones, ABBA, etc". Grove Music Online also states that ". in the early 1960s, (the term) ' pop music ' competed terminologically with beat music (in England), while in the USA its coverage overlapped (as it still does) with that of ' rock and roll '".
[0.040, 0.218, 0.563] Agent refined response: Pop has its roots in a number of different styles of music, including early forms of rock and roll, doo-wop, and Motown, and sometimes includes catchy hooks and/or riffs. Some of the most popular pop music today (often termed "pop-rock") has roots in the rock and roll of the 1950s, 1960s and 1970s. Some have argued that the term "pop" has come to refer to a more general trend of popular music, rather than a particular genre, with some genres such as hip-hop and electronic dance music having become the most popular forms of music in the 21st century.

The United States Declaration of Independence is a statement of political philosophy which describes the rights and principles that the Thirteen Colonies in North America stated to be the reason for seeking independence from Great Britain. The Declaration has had a profound impact upon political philosophy, and is a key document in the history of democracy.

Prompt: when did equus first appear in fossil record
Ground truth: The first Old World equid fossil was found in the gypsum quarries in Montmartre, Paris, in the 1820s. The tooth was sent to the Paris Conservatory, where it was identified by Georges Cuvier, who identified it as a browsing equine related to the tapir. His sketch of the entire animal matched later skeletons found at the site.
[0.000, 0.177, 0.494] Agent refined response: Equus, having its origin around 45 million years ago, was not yet present in the fossil record. It was therefore not until  around 30 million years ago, during the late Miocene, that the first Equus fossils were found. The first known Equus, a species known as Equus quagga, was found in Africa (the name is derived from the Zulu word for zebra). Equus was once thought to be a distinct genus; however, it is now considered to be a subgenus of the genus Equus (E. quagga is now the earliest known member of the Equus genus). All Equus species are now extinct. Equus simus (the zebra) and Equus caballus (the wild ass) are both thought to have originated around 2.5 million years ago.

Prompt: sending money home to the native country is an example of
Ground truth: A remittance is a transfer of money by a foreign worker to an individual in his or her home country. Money sent home by migrants competes with international aid as one of the largest financial inflows to developing countries. Workers' remittances are a significant part of international capital flows, especially with regard to labour-exporting countries. In 2014, $436 billion went to developing countries, setting a new record. Overall global remittances totaled $582 billion in 2015. Some countries, such as India and China, receive tens of billions of US dollars in remittances each year from their expatriates and diaspora. In 2014, India received an estimated $70 billion and China an estimated $64 billion.
[0.124, 0.278, 0.692] Agent refined response: By sending money to an individual or an organization in his or her home country of origin. Remittances are an important part of the economy of many developing countries. In 2015, the World Bank reported that the world's migrant workers sent $436 billion to their families in their home countries. Remittances are in contrast to international aid, such as foreign aid from governments and other organizations. Remittances from diaspora communities are one of the largest contributors to foreign exchange earnings in many developing countries. In 21 countries, remittances from diaspora exceeded the total amount of foreign direct investment in 2013. Remittances are widely used as a means of transferring funds from migrant workers to their families.

Prompt: what is foreign account tax compliance act (fatca)
Ground truth: The Foreign Account Tax Compliance Act (FATCA) is a 2010 United States federal law requiring all non-U.S. (' foreign ') financial institutions (FFIs) to search their records for customers with indicia of ' U.S.-person ' status, such as a U.S. place of birth, and to report the assets and identities of such persons to the U.S. Department of the Treasury. FATCA also requires such persons to self-report their non-U.S. financial assets annually to the Internal Revenue Service (IRS) on form 8938, which is in addition to the older and further redundant requirement to self-report them annually to the Financial Crimes Enforcement Network (FinCEN) on form 114 (also known as' FBAR '). Like U.S. income tax law, FATCA applies to U.S. residents and also to U.S. citizens and green card holders residing in other countries.
[0.108, 0.292, 0.653] Agent refined response: By the Financial Account Tax Compliance Act (FATCA), signed into law on 12 March 2010 (U.S. Pub.L. 111-147 (8 U.S.C. § 1471-1474)), the U.S. government requires financial institutions to report to the U.S. Department of the Treasury information on financial accounts held by non-U.S. persons (such as foreign individuals, foreign corporations, or foreign trusts, which are also referred to as non-U.S. persons) to be taxed on their worldwide income. The information is also to be exchanged with the tax authorities of the countries of the account holders.

Prompt: when did they start vaccinating for whooping cough
Ground truth: Before vaccines, an average of 178,171 cases was reported in the U.S., with peaks reported every two to five years; more than 93% of reported cases occurred in children under 10 years of age. The actual incidence was likely much higher. After vaccinations were introduced in the 1940s, pertussis incidence fell dramatically to approximately 1,000 by 1976. Incidence rates have increased since 1980. In 2015, rates in the United States were 20,762 people.

In the 1930s scientists were able to isolate the pertussis toxin from Bordetella pertussis and began developing a vaccine that used the toxin to stimulate an immune response. Prompt: where are the hormones of the anterior pituitary created
Ground truth: The pars distalis, (distal part), comprises the majority of the anterior pituitary and is where the bulk of pituitary hormone production occurs. The pars distalis contains two types of cells including chromophobe cells and chromophil cells. The chromophils can be further divided into acidophils (alpha cells) and basophils (beta cells). These cells all together produce hormones of the anterior pituitary, and release them into the blood stream.
[0.000, 0.182, 0.564] Agent refined response: Developing, maintaining pituitary function is critical for overall body homeostasis. The anterior pituitary gland is responsible for secreting hormones in response to hypothalamic signals. This hormone secretion regulates a wide range of bodily functions, including metabolism, growth, electrolyte balance, and reproduction. The anterior pituitary secretes these hormones in response to the hypothalamus's signals. The hypothalamus acts as a regulator of hormone secretion by secreting hormones (such as gonadotropin-releasing hormone and thyrotropin-releasing hormine) which in turn stimulate the anterior pituitary to release the appropriate hormones. The anterior pituitary has a high concentration of cells with a specific pattern of gene expression. The gene expression pattern of these cells is established during development and is maintained through cellular differentiation.

Prompt: to whom do the powers not given to the federal government by the constitution go
Ground truth: The Tenth Amendment (Amendment X) to the United States Constitution, which is part of the Bill of Rights, was ratified on December 15, 1791. It expresses the principle of federalism and states' rights, which strictly supports the entire plan of the original Constitution for the United States of America, by stating that the federal government possesses only those powers delegated to it by the United States Constitution. All remaining powers are reserved for the states or the people.
[0.000, 0.254, 0.602] Agent refined response: By the U.S. Constitution it is the doctrine of the separation of powers. The Constitution was divided into three branches of government: the legislative, executive and judicial. The Constitution provides that each branch of government was to be separate and independent of the others. This was intended to prevent any one branch of government from becoming too powerful. Prompt: what is the full scientific name for the e. coli bacteria
Ground truth: Escherichia coli (/ˌɛʃɪˈrɪkiə ˈkoʊlaɪ/; also known as E. coli) is a Gram-negative, facultatively anaerobic, rod-shaped, coliform bacterium of the genus Escherichia that is commonly found in the lower intestine of warm-blooded organisms (endotherms). Most E. coli strains are harmless, but some serotypes can cause serious food poisoning in their hosts, and are occasionally responsible for product recalls due to food contamination. The harmless strains are part of the normal flora of the gut, and can benefit their hosts by producing vitamin K, and preventing colonization of the intestine with pathogenic bacteria, having a symbiotic relationship. E. coli is expelled into the environment within fecal matter. The bacterium grows massively in fresh fecal matter under aerobic conditions for 3 days, but its numbers decline slowly afterwards.
[0.103, 0.287, 0.635] Agent refined response: Escherichia coli E1 coli also E. coli abbreviation 103 serotype O157 with  strain K-12. Genus Escherichia and species Escherichia coli. Phylum Proteobacteria, family Enterobacteriaceae and order Enterobacterales. First identified in 1885 by German bacteriologist Paul Ehrlich, Escherichia coli is a gram-negative, rod-shaped, facultative anaerobic bacterium that is commonly found in the lower intestine of warm-blooded animals, and is a major cause of food poisoning. E. coli O157 is a serotype of E. coli that is commonly found in the intestines of cattle.

Mendeleev was assisted by his brother Ivan who helped him compile his table. His work laid foundation for modern chemistry. Mendeleev published his table in 1869. However his table was not complete until 1871 when he included all 66 known elements. His table contained all known elements until 1919.

Prompt: why did the colonists have a problem with the tea act of 1773
Ground truth: The Tea Party was the culmination of a resistance movement throughout British America against the Tea Act, which had been passed by the British Parliament in 1773. Colonists objected to the Tea Act because they believed that it violated their rights as Englishmen to "No taxation without representation", that is, to be taxed only by their own elected representatives and not by a British parliament in which they were not represented. In addition, the well-connected East India Company had been granted competitive advantages over colonial tea importers, who resented the move and feared additional infringement on their business. Protesters had successfully prevented the unloading of taxed tea in three other colonies, but in Boston, embattled Royal Governor Thomas Hutchinson refused to allow the tea to be returned to Britain.
[0.069, 0.217, 0.617] Agent refined response: In 1773 the British Parliament passed the Tea Act because it wanted to help its "loaned" colonies in America pay their debts to British merchants, and it wanted to ensure that the colonies would not be able to trade freely with other countries. The British government had long held the right to tax the colonies, but it had not previously taxed the tea they imported. The Tea Act, however, imposed a tax on all tea consumed in the colonies, and it allowed the only authorized importer of tea to be the British East India Company, which was a monopoly and had been the main importer of tea to the colonies.

Prompt: how long did it take to build the great pyramid of egypt
Ground truth: Egyptologists believe the pyramid was built as a tomb for the Fourth Dynasty Egyptian pharaoh Khufu (often Hellenized as "Cheops") and was constructed over a 20-year period. Khufu's vizier, Hemiunu (also called Hemon) is believed by some to be the architect of the Great Pyramid. It is thought that, at construction, the Great Pyramid was originally 280 Egyptian Royal cubits tall (146.5 metres (480.6 ft)), but with erosion and absence of its pyramidion, its present height is 138.8 metres (455.4 ft). Each base side was 440 cubits, 230.4 metres (755.9 ft) long. The mass of the pyramid is estimated at 5.9 million tonnes. The volume, including an internal hillock, is roughly 2,500,000 cubic metres (88,000,000 cu ft).
[0.000, 0.196, 0.593] Agent refined response: The construction of the pyramid by the pharaoh Khufu also led to a series of architectural innovations. Its base,  with a perimeter of about 1,000 meters, is a square of 230 meters side, with a height of  481 meters. The pyramid took approximately 20 years to build, and required the work of 100,000 workers, mostly skilled craftsmen, engineers, and laborers. The Great Pyramid of Giza, built for the pharaoh Khufu, was the largest of the pyramids. It contains an estimated 2.3 million stone blocks, each weighing an average of 2.5 tons.

Prompt: where do the secretory cells of endocrine glands secrete their products
Ground truth: Exocrine glands are glands that produce and secrete substances onto an epithelial surface by way of a duct. Examples of exocrine glands include sweat, salivary, mammary, ceruminous, lacrimal, sebaceous, and mucous. Exocrine glands are one of two types of glands in the human body, the other being endocrine glands, which secrete their products directly into the bloodstream. The liver and pancreas are both exocrine and endocrine glands; they are exocrine glands because they secrete products--bile and pancreatic juice--into the gastrointestinal tract through a series of ducts, and endocrine because they secrete other substances directly into the bloodstream.
[0.000, 0.166, 0.640] Agent refined response: Secretory cells--endocrine cells--secrete their products into bloodstreams, rather than urine. The products of endocrine cells are chemical messengers that help regulate various physiological processes. The secretory cells secrete their products into the bloodstreams that are close to the endocrine glands. The hormones secreted by the endocrine cells pass through the bloodstreams to the target cells, where they bind to the receptors. The hormones include thyroid hormones (thyroxine and triiodothyronine), insulin, growth hormone, oxytocin, prolactin, and adrenocorticotropic hormone. The endocrine cells include thyroid gland, parathyroid gland, adrenal gland, pancreas, pituitary gland, and ovary.

As many as 60 conspirators, led by Brutus and Cassius, were involved. According to Plutarch, a seer had warned that harm would come to Caesar no later than the Ides of March. This meeting is famously dramatised in William Shakespeare's play Julius Caesar, when Caesar is warned by the soothsayer to "beware the Ides of March." The Roman biographer Suetonius identifies the "seer" as a haruspex named Spurinna.

Ground truth: The Beer Hall Putsch, also known as the Munich Putsch, and, in German, as the Hitlerputsch, Hitler-Ludendorff-Putsch, Bürgerbräu-Putsch or mostly Marsch auf die Feldherrnhalle, was a failed coup attempt by the Nazi Party (NSDAP) leader Adolf Hitler--along with Generalquartiermeister Erich Ludendorff and other Kampfbund leaders--to seize power in Munich, Bavaria, during 8--9 November 1923. About two thousand Nazis marched to the centre of Munich, where they confronted the police, which resulted in the death of 16 Nazis and four police officers. Hitler was not wounded during the clash, although he locked his left arm with the right arm of Max Erwin von Scheubner-Richter who, when he was shot and killed, pulled Hitler to the pavement with him. Hitler escaped immediate arrest and was spirited off to safety in the countryside. After two days, Hitler was arrested and charged with treason.

The event occurred on November 8 and 9, 1923. The Nazi Party headquarters was in a large beer hall called "Bavarian Bierkeller". Prompt: when does a dieback or population crash occur
Ground truth: In population dynamics and population ecology, overshoot occurs when a population temporarily exceeds the long term carrying capacity of its environment. The environment usually has mechanisms in place to prevent overshoot. For example, plants are only able to regenerate and regrow a few times after being consumed before completely dying off. The consequence of overshoot is called a collapse, a crash or a die-off in which there is a decline in population density. The entire sequence or trajectory undergone by the population and its environment together is often termed ' overshoot-and-collapse '.
[0.000, 0.209, 0.581] Agent refined response: Dieback means the process of the decline. In some cases, a species ' population may be reduced by a significant amount, or even extirpated. The mechanism is thought to be a result of environmental stress and genetic drift. A population crash can occur when the number of individuals in a population declines rapidly and often to the point of extinction. The reasons are usually natural and can be due to the species ' ability to adapt to its environment and the result of competition and predation. The decline can be caused by factors such as habitat destruction or fragmentation and overhunting. A population crash can occur in a single generation or may be a result of a long-term decline.

Prompt: what are the 3 layers of the meninges
Ground truth: The meninges (/məˈnɪndʒiːz/, singular: meninx (/ˈmiːnɪŋks/or/ˈmɛnɪŋks/), from Ancient Greek: μῆνιγξ, translit. mēninx, lit. ' membrane ', adjectival: meningeal/məˈnɪndʒəl/) are the three membranes that envelop the brain and spinal cord. In mammals, the meninges are the dura mater, the arachnoid mater, and the pia mater. Cerebrospinal fluid is located in the subarachnoid space between the arachnoid mater and the pia mater. The primary function of the meninges is to protect the central nervous system.
[0.148, 0.284, 0.572] Agent refined response: The three layers of the meninges are the dura mater (outermost), arachnoid mater (middle) and pia mater (innermost). The dura mater is a tough, leathery membrane of collagen and elastin. The arachnoid mater is a thin, vascular membrane of collagen and elastic fibers. The pia mater is a delicate, vascular membrane of the brain and spinal cord. The meninges are responsible for protecting the brain and spinal cord, and are involved in the exchange of nutrients and waste products. In the case of meningitis, the meninges become inflamed, which can cause fever, headache, and the sensitivity to light.

Most areas of the United States observe daylight saving time (DST), the exceptions being Arizona (except for the Navajo, who do observe daylight saving time on tribal lands), Hawaii, and the overseas territories of American Samoa, Guam, the Northern Mariana Islands, Puerto Rico, and the United States Virgin Islands. The Uniform Time Act of 1966 established the system of uniform Daylight Saving Time throughout the US.

Prompt: where are the cones in the eye located
Ground truth: Cone cells, or cones, are one of three types of photoreceptor cells in the retina of mammalian eyes (e.g. the human eye). They are responsible for color vision and function best in relatively bright light, as opposed to rod cells, which work better in dim light. Cone cells are densely packed in the fovea centralis, a 0.3 mm diameter rod-free area with very thin, densely packed cones which quickly reduce in number towards the periphery of the retina. There are about six to seven million cones in a human eye and are most concentrated towards the macula. The commonly cited figure of six million cone cells in the human eye was found by Osterberg in 1935. Oyster's textbook (1999) cites work by Curcio et al. (1990) indicating an average close to 4.5 million cone cells and 90 million rod cells in the human retina.
[0.000, 0.174, 0.578] Agent refined response: Cones, like most other photoreceptors, are distributed across the retina, but are most numerous in the central part, where the visual acuity is highest. They have a characteristic bipolar shape, with two cell bodies located at the retinal pigment epithelium. Because of their position, cones are able to detect light more efficiently than rods, whereas rods are more sensitive to low light levels. It is believed that the rod cells are able to sense the direction of the light, whereas the cone cells are able to sense the color of the light.

Prompt: when does april fools day end at noon
Ground truth: In Poland, prima aprilis ("1 April" in Latin) as a day of jokes is a centuries-long tradition. It is a day in which many jokes are told; various hoaxes--sometimes very sophisticated--are prepared by people, media (which often cooperate to make the "information" more credible) and even public institutions. Serious activities are usually avoided, and generally every word said on April 1st can be a lie, or a joke. The conviction for this is so strong that the Polish anti-Turkish alliance with Leopold I signed on April 1, 1683, was backdated to March 31. However, for some in Poland prima aprilis ends at noon of April 1st, and prima aprilis jokes after that hour are considered inappropriate and not classy.

In others (e.g., the United States), the day is not widely observed. The celebrations are usually short, usually lasting only a few minutes, although some people might be fooled for longer periods. The idea is to try to fool people, usually by telling them something which is untrue.

Prompt: where is connective tissue found in the body
Ground truth: Connective tissue (CT) is one of the four basic types of animal tissue, along with epithelial tissue, muscle tissue, and nervous tissue. It develops from the mesoderm. Connective tissue is found in between other tissues everywhere in the body, including the nervous system. In the central nervous system, the three outer membranes (the meninges) that envelop the brain and spinal cord are composed of connective tissue. They support and protect the body. All connective tissue consists of three main components: fibers (elastic and collagenous fibers), ground substance and cells. Not all authorities include blood or lymph as connective tissue because they lack the fiber component. All are immersed in the body water.
[0.000, 0.213, 0.612] Agent refined response: In the human body, connective tissue includes the largest tissue group. The main function of connective tissue is to support the body. Connective tissue includes the following: (1) Bone and cartilage. Bone tissue is composed of two types of bone: compact bone and spongy bone. Compact bone is dense and hard (it is the outer portion of bones). Spongy bone is porous and spongy. Bone tissue provides mechanical support, stores calcium, and releases it as needed. Cartilage is a more flexible tissue than bone. There are two types of cartilage: hyaline cartilage and elastic cartilage.

Prompt: what is the approximate volume of the brain
Ground truth: Studies on human brain size, largely based on participants of European ancestry, tend to find an average adult brain volume of 1260 cubic centimeters (cm) for men and 1130 cm for women. There is, however, substantial variation between individuals; one study of 46 adults, aged 22--49 years and of mainly European descent, found an average brain volume of 1273.6 cm for men, with a range of 1052.9 to 1498.5 cm, and 1131.1 cm for women, with a range of 974.9 to 1398.1 cm.
[0.000, 0.162, 0.572] Agent refined response: Human brain studies suggest that adult brains tend to have some very consistent average sizes. For example the human brain has been found to be on average : 1295 cubic centimeters in volume, or about 1. 4 liters. This is notably smaller than the 1500 cubic centimeters of the average human skull, with the cerebral cavity of the brain taking up only about 75 % of the available space. This volume, however, can vary greatly between individuals, with some brains being much larger or smaller than average. There is also evidence that brain size is not the only factor, and that the actual volume of brain tissue is also important.

Prompt: the elements in each period have the same number of
Ground truth: A period in the periodic table is one of the horizontal rows, all of those elements have the same number of electron shells. Going across a period, each element has one more proton and is less metallic than its predecessor. Arranged this way, groups of elements in the same column have similar chemical and physical properties, reflecting the periodic law. For example, the alkali metals lie in the first column (group 1) and share similar properties, such as high reactivity and the tendency to lose one electron to arrive at a noble-gas electronic configuration. As of 2016 a total of 118 elements have been discovered and confirmed.
[0.000, 0.145, 0.541] Agent refined response: electrons move freely between energy levels share electrons with other atoms but are limited to one noble gas per energy level, with a maximum of eight electrons in the outermost energy level (the valence shell). Atoms with more than eight electrons in the valence shell are considered to be charged, with a charge of + 1 for each additional electron; atoms with fewer than eight electrons are considered to be uncharged. The eight noble gases are: helium neon argon krypton xenon radon and the noble gas atoms are inert in general, with no known tendency to react with other elements.

Prompt: when does the heart develop and begin pumping blood
Ground truth: Heart development refers to the prenatal development of the human heart. This begins with the formation of two endocardial tubes which merge to form the tubular heart, also called the primitive heart tube, that loops and septates into the four chambers and paired arterial trunks that form the adult heart. The heart is the first functional organ in vertebrate embryos, and in the human, beats spontaneously by week 4 of development.
[0.049, 0.244, 0.623] Agent refined response: Before birth (and first heartbeat) and development, the heart initially starts with a tube like structure, called the atrioventricular (first) cavity. This first cavity forms at the junction of the two upper chambers (atria) and the two lower chambers (ventricles). The atrioventricular cavity develops from the fusion of two endocardial tubes that are connected to the primordial atria and ventricles. The first heartbeat and contraction of the heart occurs at approximately 4 weeks of gestation, and the heart begins to pump blood and establishes the cardiac loop, which connects the atria and ventricles.

In 2007, the New York Court of Appeals ruled that lethal injection was constitutional, leading to the state officially switching to the method. A second opinion, issued by the court in 2008, ruled that the state could not use lethal injection.

Prompt: what was the religion in the virginia colony
Ground truth: In the 1740s, the established Anglican church had about 70 parish priests around the colony. There was no bishop, and indeed, there was fierce political opposition to having a bishop in the colony. The Anglican priests were supervised directly by the Bishop of London. Each county court gave tax money to the local vestry, composed of prominent laymen. The vestry provided the priest a glebe of 200 or 300 acres (1.2 km), a house, and perhaps some livestock. The vestry paid him an annual salary of 16,000 pounds-of-tobacco, plus 20 shillings for every wedding and funeral. While not poor, the priests lived modestly and their opportunities for improvement were slim.
[0.000, 0.196, 0.558] Agent refined response: Established in 1607, they had no formal religion, but they had a few Anglican ministers. In 1619, the General Court of the Virginia Colony established the Church of England as the official state church, but the Church of England was not the dominant religion. The Church of England (Anglican) had a few adherents and the colony had a few Anglican clergy, but there was no attempt to impose it on the inhabitants of the colony. Ground truth: Originally written in Spanish, the book is more commonly published and read in the Philippines in either Tagalog or English. Together with its sequel, El Filibusterismo, the reading of Noli is obligatory for high school students throughout the country.
[0.000, 0.109, 0.530] Agent refined response: Jose Rizal was not the author of the novel Noli Me Tangere, but he was the author of El Filibusterismo, and the latter was completed by him after he was imprisoned by the Spanish authorities. He was inspired to write the novel after reading Ferdinand Blumentritt's notes on the history of the Philippines, and he spent some time writing it while imprisoned. Rizal was a reformist who sought to emancipate the Philippines from Spanish colonial rule. After finishing the novel, he gave a copy to a friend who sent it to a printer in Hong Kong. The novel was published in serial form from 1887 to 1889.

Prompt: when do you get a dd form 214
Ground truth: The DD Form 214, Certificate of Release or Discharge from Active Duty, generally referred to as a "DD 214", is a document of the United States Department of Defense, issued upon a military service member's retirement, separation, or discharge from active duty in the Armed Forces of the United States, e.g., U.S. Air Force, U.S. Army, U.S. Coast Guard, U.S. Marine Corps, or U.S. Navy.
[0.062, 0.178, 0.553] Agent refined response: The official DD Form 214 is the most widely-used document for the official proof of military service. It is issued to all honorably discharged veterans. There are three different types of DD Form 214--DD Form 214, DD Form 214-1 and DD Form 214 1-2. The DD Form 214, and DD Form 215 are the most commonly used forms, and the DD Form 214 (1950) is the only form in use from 1950 to 1954. The form is issued by the Department of Defense and is a prerequisite for many military and civilian benefits.

Prompt: who is tinker air force base named after
Ground truth: The base, originally known as Midwest Air Depot, is named in honor of Oklahoma native Major General Clarence L. Tinker, the first Native American Major General.
[0.052, 0.256, 0.605] Agent refined response: When the air base was selected for the installation of the Tinker Field, it was named Tinker Field. The air base was originally known as the 58th Field Depot, established on 1 January 1942. On 10 January 1942, the depot was renamed to Tinker Field in honor of Major Clarence L. Tinker, former commanding officer of the 33d Pursuit Group, killed in action on 5 May 1942. The depot was officially named Tinker Field on 15 January 1942. Major Tinker was posthumously awarded the Medal of Honor on 22 January 1942.
